Are Insiders Cashing In on Trump’s Iran Talk? | Pivot

Pivot with Kara Swisher and Scott Galloway(230.0K) ·62:30 ·85.6K views · 2026-03-27
Kara Swisher (Host, Pivot) Scott Galloway (Host, Pivot)
President Trump is stacking a new AI advisory council with conflicted Big Tech billionaires while allegedly leveraging unverified Iran "negotiations" to trigger market volatility, as landmark court rulings against Meta and YouTube signal a shift towa...
TL;DR President Trump is stacking a new AI advisory council with conflicted Big Tech billionaires while allegedly leveraging unverified Iran "negotiations" to trigger market volatility, as landmark court rulings against Meta and YouTube signal a shift toward corporate liability...

TL;DR

President Trump is stacking a new AI advisory council with conflicted Big Tech billionaires while allegedly leveraging unverified Iran "negotiations" to trigger market volatility, as landmark court rulings against Meta and YouTube signal a shift toward corporate liability for social media addiction.

AI Policy and Tech Leadership

  • The AI Council Stacking: President Trump plans to appoint Mark Zuckerberg (Meta), Larry Ellison (Oracle), and Jensen Huang (NVIDIA) to a technology council to advise on AI policy.
      • Criticism of Conflicts: Speakers argue the council lacks diverse viewpoints and is rife with self-interest; for example, Jensen Huang seeks to sell chips to China while Larry Ellison prioritizes data center expansion.
      • Policy Concerns: The briefing notes that the expected recommendation from this group will likely be "no regulation" and increased government procurement of their specific products.
  • Clarifying the Ellison Controversy: Kara Swisher addressed recent headlines regarding her comments at Syracuse University.
      • Context of "Terrible Person" Comment: Swisher clarified it was a joke directed at Scott MacFarlane; while she respects Ellison’s innovation and aesthetic, she maintains a strict policy against working for tech moguls to preserve journalistic independence.

Geopolitics and Allegations of Market Manipulation

  • Middle East Escalation: The Pentagon is deploying 2,000 troops from the 82nd Airborne Division to the Middle East. Trump claims Iran is ready for a deal involving a "big present" related to oil and gas, though Iran disputes this.
  • Insider Trading Concerns: Scott Galloway posits that the Trump administration is engaging in "insider trading on a massive scale" by making unverified claims that swing markets.
      • Market Volatility: Trump’s statements on "successful talks" cause market spikes, which then reverse when the claims are debunked, creating opportunities for those with advance knowledge to trade zero-day options.
  • Diplomatic Preferences: Reports suggest Iran prefers negotiating with VP J.D. Vance over Jared Kushner or Steve Witkoff, as Vance has historically expressed opposition to foreign "quagmires."
  • Prediction Market Regulation: Lawmakers are introducing bipartisan bills to ban prediction markets from listing sports bets and to prohibit members of Congress from trading in specific markets to curb "out-and-out grift."

Domestic Politics and the DHS Shutdown

  • Florida Election Upsets: Democrats recently flipped two legislative seats in Florida, including District 87 (which covers Mar-a-Lago).
      • Key Win: Emily Gretchery, a first-time candidate focusing on maternal health, won by two points in a district Trump previously carried by double digits.
  • DHS/TSA Standoff: Negotiations to end the five-week Department of Homeland Security shutdown are stalled over ICE funding and enforcement reforms.
      • Delta’s "Egalitarian" Move: Delta Airlines has suspended specialty security services for members of Congress, forcing lawmakers to wait in standard TSA lines alongside the public. The hosts praised this as a "brand-enhancing, brilliant move."

Tech Industry Accountability and Shifts

  • Social Media Liability: A California jury found Meta and YouTube liable for harming a young user through addictive features like infinite scroll and algorithmic recommendations.
      • Financial Penalties: While the current payouts are relatively small (Meta must pay $4.2M; YouTube $1.8M), the verdict establishes a significant legal precedent for civil liability.
      • New Mexico Verdict: Meta was also ordered to pay $375M in a separate case for failing to protect minors from online predators.
  • The Rise of Anthropic: OpenAI is reportedly losing enterprise ground to Anthropic.
      • Market Share: While 60% of new enterprise AI dollars previously went to OpenAI, that figure has dropped to 30%, with Anthropic capturing 70% of the incremental spend.
      • OpenAI Focus: OpenAI is shuttering its "Sora" video app to refocus on robotics and high-end enterprise tools.
  • Amazon’s "Transformer" Phone: Reports indicate Amazon is internally developing an AI-driven smartphone. Galloway suggests this could succeed if bundled as a "Prime Plus" benefit, potentially including satellite connectivity via Project Kuiper.

What Tesla and SpaceX Teach Founders About Building Hardware | a16z

a16z(258.0K) ·50:25 ·13.4K views · 2026-03-27
Erin Price-Wright (Partner, a16z) Chandler Luzsicza (Founder and CEO, Galadyne) Turner Caldwell (Cofounder and CEO, Mariana Minerals)
Success in complex hardware startups is driven by maximizing decision velocity through flat organizational structures and applying a rigorous "factory mindset" to every stage of development, from R&D to large-scale infrastructure construction.
TL;DR Success in complex hardware startups is driven by maximizing decision velocity through flat organizational structures and applying a rigorous "factory mindset" to every stage of development, from R&D to large-scale infrastructure construction. Information...

TL;DR

Success in complex hardware startups is driven by maximizing decision velocity through flat organizational structures and applying a rigorous "factory mindset" to every stage of development, from R&D to large-scale infrastructure construction.

Organizational Speed and Decision Velocity

  • Information Democratization: Maintain flat organizations not just for culture, but to ensure rapid information flow. Any junior engineer should be able to speak directly to executives to prevent data silos.
  • High-Conviction Leadership: Velocity is maintained by leaders who make fast decisions without waiting for 100% of the information.
      • Risk Absolution: When leaders take ownership of high-stakes decisions, it removes the "fear of failure" from junior engineers, allowing them to execute faster.
      • Iterative Betting: Accept that not every decision will be correct; the goal is to maximize the percentage of right bets while iterating rapidly on the wrong ones.
  • The "Data Backbone": Use integrated systems (web apps, LLMs) to track the history of every decision. This provides context to the entire team, allowing them to make "globally optimal" decisions rather than localized ones.

Critical Path and Factory Mindsets

  • Critical Path Management: Identify the specific "schedule-driving" task or procurement activity that blocks the next phase.
      • Avoid "Second-Grade Soccer": Do not allow the entire team to swarm a single problem. Use small "SWAT teams" to attack the critical path while keeping parallel tasks moving.
  • Manufacturing Principles in Construction: Treat refineries and mines like factories by using Takt Time Analysis—breaking down every discrete step (e.g., torqueing bolts, processing samples) into a quantifiable manufacturing task.
  • Short-Interval Control: Implement manufacturing-style dashboards for construction and R&D.
      • Shift Pass-downs: Use end-of-day reports ("What we did vs. what we were supposed to do") to maintain a constant drumbeat.
      • Automation: Auto-populate data reports so humans focus on commentary and accountability rather than manual data entry.
  • Requirement Questioning: Aggressively "delete" requirements that sound illogical. Simple designs are faster and cheaper to produce, whereas bespoke requirements often lead to production bottlenecks.

Strategic Vertical Integration

  • Binary Decision Making: Vertical integration should not be a romantic ideal but a strategic necessity. Only integrate if the answer to "Does the company exist if we don't do this?" is "No."
      • Drivers for Integration: Lack of existing technology, prohibitive third-party costs, or a specific component acting as a permanent production bottleneck.
  • The Risk Transfer Trap: Founders must recognize that vertical integration doesn’t eliminate a supply chain; it absorbs it. You become responsible for the upstream risks and sub-components previously managed by a vendor.
  • Cost vs. Capability: In the early stages, integration should be driven by capability. Cost-driven integration only becomes relevant once the company is focused on driving down unit costs at scale.

Hiring for High-Talent Density

  • Technical Rigor: Implement an exhaustive hiring process (6–10 interviews and deep-dive technical tests). This filters for "cracked" engineers who are motivated by working with high-caliber peers.
  • The Internship Engine: Use internship programs as a 3-month trial period. At SpaceX, "intern conversions" make up a significant portion of the core engineering team because they have already proven they can "crush" difficult tasks.
  • Mission Alignment as a Burnout Shield: Long hours and all-nighters are sustainable only when the team is deeply aligned with a "fiery" mission (e.g., multi-planetary life or national defense).
      • Progress vs. Churn: Burnout is caused more by "churn" (politics, data silos, and erratic decision-making) than by hard work. Seeing tangible progress keeps engineers motivated.

Notable Quotes

"Simple is fast, simple is cheap. That part of the SpaceX mantra is very real... questioning every requirement enables an engineer to design a very simple solution." — Chandler Luzsicza
"The thing that actually causes burnout is churn and a lack of feeling like you're making progress towards a goal... solving hard problems can be fun as long as you are making progress." — Turner Caldwell
"You should only be vertically integrating into the things that have a binary outcome of: the company exists or the company doesn't exist." — Turner Caldwell
"If a leader can come in and remove concern from a junior engineer's mind by just making the decision and saying 'Go,' then you go way, way faster." — Chandler Luzsicza

People talk about the mythology—the all-nighters, the flat org, the impossible deadlines, "the best part is no part." So a little bit of V1 play in terms of full stack, and then pushed all the way through the V2 development cycle and then started to touch V3 and kind of lay that out before I left the company. I think really what this trade for us came down to is—and what enabled kind of the speed and thinking with this factory mindset is—and really just production focus in general—is requirements. From the design side, if you can boil up all of the requirements that sound stupid as fast as possible and then give yourself a chance to whack them out of the equation, you now leave yourself... Like what? Give me an example if you can. Yeah, there is one that's kind of interesting, it's kind of getting into the weeds but... we had the opportunity to pull some hardware from booster. Booster was a little bit ahead of the ship team in terms of their V3 design, they kind of skipped V2 for booster so they went from V1 to V3 vehicle. And they had some hardware design for the V3 vehicle that actually could have been plugged into ship very easily. We had very limited engineers to go design a ton of prop systems hardware and we identified that oh, we could maybe use this. We kind of brought it over early, which would have been earlier implementation than booster. One of the things that popped up that sounds kind of silly is it was essentially a snorkel that lives inside the fuel tank and you could condense liquid inside that snorkel and the valves that were effectively venting the tank there don't like liquid. So when we were trying to go super fast, like "Oh sweet, we got free hardware, we can skip a whole design cycle, let's go fast and we can just jump into production sooner rather than later," we were like "Oh, we're gonna get liquid in this thing too. I don't know if the valves are gonna like that." And what we did is we just spooled up the necessary resources to go prove that that's going to be okay. What that did is not only allowed us to go roll in this whole hardware design into production sooner, but it also enabled booster once they got there to go use the same hardware. From a company goal perspective, it was fantastic and it was the perfect direction. I think I won't even take credit for all of that ideation, actually another guy on the team who's still there kind of brought it to my plate and I'm like "Oh man, that's freaking great. Let's do it. Let's go fast." But that's one thing and it's really just questioning every requirement because what that does is it enables your requirement set whenever an engineer steps in to go actually design the hardware to be enabled to design a very simple solution. And simple is fast, simple is cheap. Really I think that whole part of the SpaceX mantra is very real and it is happening across Starship, it's even happening on fixes on Dragon as well. Like if you hadn't been focused—if you hadn't been thinking two steps ahead about production, you would have just designed something from scratch that was perfectly bespoke to the set of requirements. Exactly, bespoke requirements. And I think we literally had a whole design for all this hardware before realizing "Oh crap, we should just go use that." Because then we can go start figuring out how to build these somewhat complex weldments sooner rather than later and then build the production of those very quickly. That's where information access really matters. Absolutely. What about you, Turner? You know, you helped build Tesla's billion-dollar lithium refinery in Corpus Christi, which is now up and running. What were some of the hardest challenges you overcame? How did you approach them with this kind of factory and production mindset? Yeah, I think that—so design for manufacturing is kind of how you do product design and ensure that you can scale that into something that can be produced at scale with very short takt times and all that. But I mean people don't really think about a refinery as a product. Exactly. And so what we spent a good amount of time thinking about and then obviously we thought about when I was at Tesla was when you're in construction basically, the environment is you're building a custom thing, right? And so you have to break that down into modular subsets that can be manufactured offsite and brought to site. Then you have to think like everything should have a takt time analysis associated with it, right? A what analysis? A takt time analysis, which is basically breaking down all the discrete steps required to build something effectively. And that needs to happen... Is that a Tesla/SpaceX term or is that an industry term? That's an industry term. Okay. Yeah, can't take credit for inventing takt time analysis. The key there is that through the whole value chain, from R&D and how your analytical labs run, you should be thinking about okay, what are the individual steps that go from a sample comes into a lab to a result comes out. You need to run analytical labs like little manufacturing processes. On construction, you need to boil it down into specific subsets of tasks that are actually torqueing bolts and actually welding out seams to build that up into what is the schedule actually need to be instead of doing top-down assessments of kind of how long something is going to take to build. And then when you're in mining operations, you also need to do super discrete takt time analyses of all of the discrete tasks that happen around a mining operation. I think that the construction industry does not—some folks do this, but because of the custom nature of each individual project, it takes time and effort and you have to allocate resources to be able to build from the ground up of how long it actually takes to do something. As we start to think about how do you run construction like manufacturing operations, the way a construction site runs typically is a superintendent has been given their target for the month. On a daily basis they'll go out to the field, they'll stand in a circle with the trades, they'll say "What are you doing today? What are you doing today?" At the end of the day they come back, they say "What did you do today?" and there isn't a lot of quantification that happens on a regular basis. There isn't a lot of short-interval control of construction operations that is actually quantified. And so what we've started doing at Mariana is breaking that down, and this ends up being like a data management thing and a resource allocation optimization that you have to do where you have a subset of materials and equipment that are on-site, you have a subset of people that are on-site, and you have a set of tasks to be done. Today that is like humans trying to sit between those three databases and decide what is the task that is going to happen at the site. And we see a ton of opportunity there to do that algorithmically that enables you, if you have all of that data available, you can actually set daily, hourly goals. Then if you can automate the data capture that is happening at the site—like Boston Dynamics has the Spot dog that works great, it can kind of roam around the site, take 3D scans. You have to do a lot of work on the software side to be able to reconcile the 3D scans that have come in to the model. Then you can actually do short-interval control on construction, which is effectively what manufacturing is, which is super short interval control. Everyone has a dashboard that tells you "Okay, how many parts are we trying to make today at this station?" and they know whether they are trending towards their goal or they are trending behind their goal or exceeding their goal. And measuring the things that matter in construction, in mining, in refining—that is actually the cultural thing that needs to shift and that we did a good amount of, tried to do a good amount of, at Tesla, but there's ultimately you have to invest in the software backbone that enables that. Few founders I've heard as often "we are ahead of schedule and under budget" than you, Turner. Well, we're trying. It's about measuring and quantifying. I think a lot of manufacturing—you go really deep on understanding because you're making thousands of parts an hour depending on the scale of manufacturing process. That level of measuring and measuring against goals and setting targets just doesn't happen in this large-scale infrastructure ecosystem. Maybe talking a little bit about vertical integration. We're coming from places that have made it look easy for sure, but it's not. It's really not easy from the outside. It's a very romantic dream. Absolutely. Yeah, I don't think vertically integrating for the point of vertically integrating, to kind of echo what Chandler was saying, makes any sense. I think every vertical integration decision—which there are thousands of them—need to boil down to one question, especially in the early days of companies: does the company exist or not if you make the decision to vertically—if you don't make the decision to vertically integrate into the thing? If you boil it down to a subset of problems that are binary in "Does Mariana exist or Mariana not exist?", that makes the decision easy where—and that could be a number of drivers. It's either the part doesn't exist, the technology doesn't exist, or the cost is so insanely high that the company can't exist by not vertically integrating into the thing. And so if the—and I call it the thing because there's many, many things and sub-components or parts of the software stack that, if you don't go and do it yourself, the company just won't exist. In the early days when you have resources that are slim and you have to decide where do I allocate this team that needs to continue to grow, you should only be vertically integrating into the things that have a binary outcome of "the company exists or the company doesn't exist." Then eventually once you start to have a large team that can go and you're driving down unit cost, that's where the cost-driven vertical integration starts to become an interesting and much longer decision, I would say. Because now the risk transfer from a supplier to yourself is the main thing that you have to be thinking about, right? The folks that do anything in your supply chain, they are carrying risk in those activities, right? You're not eliminating a supply chain point, you're actually expanding your supply chain interactions because if you vertically integrate into something that's upstream, they have their own supply chain that you now have to absorb. We made the decision to go and be a software company that builds and operates minerals infrastructure mainly because software companies that sell to mining companies have a very, very hard time penetrating the sector because the issue is the rate of uptake of software and of technology is actually gated by what would be the customers if you were a pure play SaaS company. And so the question for us in that regard was always: does Mariana exist or not if we're not both a software company and a mining company? The answer to that was no, the company would not exist. François Chollet: Why Scaling Alone Isn’t Enough for AGI

Y Combinator(2.2M) ·57:24 ·12.9K views · 2026-03-27
François Chollet (Creator of the ARC prize)
François Chollet argues that true AGI requires a paradigm shift from data-hungry deep learning to "program synthesis"—creating compact, symbolic models that prioritize human-like learning efficiency over massive scaling.
TL;DR François Chollet argues that true AGI requires a paradigm shift from data-hungry deep learning to "program synthesis"—creating compact, symbolic models that prioritize human-like learning efficiency over massive scaling. He predicts AGI by 2030, likely emerging as a...

TL;DR

François Chollet argues that true AGI requires a paradigm shift from data-hungry deep learning to "program synthesis"—creating compact, symbolic models that prioritize human-like learning efficiency over massive scaling. He predicts AGI by 2030, likely emerging as a surprisingly small codebase (under 10,000 lines) focused on "agentic intelligence" rather than just pattern matching.

Ndea and the Shift to Symbolic Learning

  • A New ML Paradigm: Chollet’s new lab, Ndea, is building an alternative to deep learning based on "program synthesis" rather than parametric curves.
      • Symbolic vs. Gradient Descent: While deep learning uses gradient descent to fit parameters on a curve, Ndea uses "symbolic descent" to find the simplest, most concise symbolic model that explains data.
      • The Efficiency Goal: This approach aims for "optimality," requiring significantly less data for training and much less compute at inference time.
      • Generalization: Based on the minimum description length principle, smaller models generalize better. Chollet believes deep learning will eventually trend toward this symbolic optimality.

The Limits of Current Scaling and LLMs

  • Verifiable vs. Non-Verifiable Domains: Current LLM success is largely driven by domains with "verifiable reward signals," such as code and mathematics.
      • The Automation Loop: In verifiable domains, models can generate their own training data via trial and error (RL loops), leading to rapid automation.
      • The "Stall" in Reasoning: In non-verifiable domains like essay writing or law, progress will likely stall because models rely on expensive, slow-to-acquire human annotations.
  • Knowledge vs. Intelligence: Chollet distinguishes between "competency" (knowledge) and "intelligence" (the ability to learn new skills).
      • Current models are becoming more useful through better training (knowledge), but they are not necessarily becoming "smarter" in terms of fluid intelligence.

Evolution of the ARC-AGI Benchmark

  • ARC V1 & V2 (Reasoning & Coding):
      • V1: Targeted fluid intelligence. Base LLMs performed poorly until the advent of reasoning models (e.g., OpenAI’s o1/o3), which represented a "step function" change.
      • V2: Captured the rise of agentic coding. It was recently saturated (97% score) by using custom "harnesses" and RL loops to brute-force the problem space.
  • ARC V3 (Agentic Intelligence):
      • Interactive Environments: Unlike previous versions, V3 drops the agent into a "mini video game" environment with no instructions or prior data.
      • Measuring Efficiency: The agent must explore, figure out controls, and set goals on its own. Success is measured by "action efficiency"—how few moves the AI takes compared to a human.
      • Core Knowledge Priors: The games avoid cultural symbols (like language or common icons) to focus strictly on innate reasoning and physics-based logic.

The Path to AGI by 2030

  • The "10,000 Lines" Prediction: Chollet posits that AGI will retrospectively be seen as a very small codebase, possibly under 10,000 lines, that could have theoretically run on 1980s hardware if the algorithm had been known.
  • Self-Improving Systems: A key requirement for AGI is removing the "human bottleneck." The system must be able to improve its own capabilities without engineers manually adjusting the code.
  • Timeline: Chollet expects AGI around 2030 (timed with the potential release of ARC-6 or ARC-7), noting that we are currently on a trajectory to automate economically valuable work before we achieve true human-level learning efficiency.

Advice for Researchers and Developers

  • Look Back to Move Forward: Chollet suggests that aspiring researchers study AI papers from the 1970s and 80s, when a wider variety of approaches (like genetic algorithms) were explored before the field "collapsed" into deep learning.
  • Build Compounding Stacks: Labs should avoid constantly switching ideas; instead, build reusable foundations where each layer of research builds upon the last.
  • Leveraging AI as Empowerment: Rather than fearing displacement, individuals should gain deep expertise in specific domains to leverage AI tools. Expertise allows humans to "ride the wave" of accelerating progress.
"AGI is basically going to be a system that can approach any new problem, any new task, any new domain, and make sense of it... with the same degree of efficiency as a human could." — François Chollet
"Retrospectively it will turn out that [AGI] is a codebase that's less than 10,000 lines of code. And that if you had known about it back in the 1980s, you could have done AGI back then using the compute resources available back then." — François Chollet
"Science is fundamentally a symbolic compression process... Science is not about curve fitting; science is about finding the equation." — François Chollet
"You're not going to stop AI progress... The question is: How do you make use of it? How do you leverage it? How do you ride the wave?" — François Chollet
I think we're probably looking at AGI 2030, around the time that we're going to be releasing like maybe ARC-6 or ARC-7. You're not going to stop AI progress. I think it's too late for that. And so the next question is, okay, like AI progress is here, it's actually going to keep accelerating. How do you make use of it? How do you leverage? How do you ride the wave? That's the question to ask. Today we're lucky to be joined by François Chollet, founder of the ARC Prize, a global competition to solve the ARC-AGI benchmark. His latest project is Ndea, a lab exploring a new paradigm in frontier AI research. François is one of the best people in the world to help us understand the current AI moment and where all of this is going. François, thank you so much for joining us today and congrats on the launch of ARC-AGI V3. Thanks so much for having me. I'm super excited to be here. Super exciting time to talk about AI. So François, tell us a little bit about Ndea. What exactly is it and what are you guys trying to achieve? Right, so Ndea is this new AGI research lab and we're trying some very different ideas. Our goal is basically to build this new branch of machine learning that will be much closer to optimal, unlike deep learning. All of us right now are sort of taken by what's going on with code. I have sort of this viral moment right now where I got to 40,000 stars this morning on G-Stack. So it's like oh, this is an open-source project that now is one of the biggest ones and I have more than 100 PRs from contributors to deal with. I guess you're one of the best people to talk to about this because you're actually literally coming up with something that is a totally different pathway. That's right, that's right. So what we're doing at Ndea is we're doing program synthesis research. And when I talk about program synthesis, often people ask me, "Oh, so are you doing like codegen? Are you building an alternative to coding agents?" And that's actually not at all what we're doing. We're working at a much, much lower level than that. What we're actually doing is that we are trying to build a new branch of machine learning, an alternative to deep learning itself rather than like coding agents. Coding agents are like this very, very high-level last layer piece of the stack. We're actually trying to rebuild the whole stack on top of different foundations. So we're building a new learning substrate that's very different from parametric learning or deep learning. If you go back to the problem of machine learning, you have some input data and some target data. You're trying to find a function that will map the inputs to the targets and that will hopefully generalize to new inputs. If you're doing deep learning, what you're doing is that you have this parametric curve that serves as your function, as your model, and you're trying to fit the parameters of the curve via gradient descent. This is basically what we're doing, except we're replacing the parametric curve with a symbolic model that is meant to be as small as possible. It's like the simplest possible model to explain the data and to model what's going on. Of course, if you're doing that, you cannot apply gradient descent anymore. So we are building something that we call symbolic descent, which is like the symbolic space equivalent of gradient descent. The idea is to build this new machine learning engine that's giving you extremely concise symbolic models of the data you're feeding into it, and then we're going to make it scale. Everything you're doing with machine learning today with parametric curves, we should be able to do it with symbolic models in the future in a way that will be much, much closer to optimality. It is much closer to optimality in the sense that you're going to need much less data to obtain the models. The models are going to run much more efficiently at inference time because they're going to be so small. Because they're so small, they will also generalize much better and compose much better. You know the minimum description length principle: the model of the data that is most likely to generalize is the shortest. I think you cannot find a model like this if you're doing parametric learning; you need to try symbolic learning. That's fascinating. The rest of the industry is just pouring more and more billions of dollars down an approach that was set years ago. Can you help make the case for why you think that it's the right thing to explore alternate approaches instead of just keeping putting more money into the current approach? I mean everybody is building on top of the LLM stack these days, which makes sense because the returns are there. It's actually working. So it would seem very sensible for everybody to just be doing what seems to be the currently most productive path. But our take is actually that it's counterproductive to have everybody working on the same thing. I personally don't think that machine learning or AI in 50 years is still going to be built on this stack. I think this is a stack that is very nice, maybe it even gets us to AGI, but it's not as efficient as it should be. I think it's inevitable that the world of AI will trend over time towards optimality. And so I'm trying to sort of leapfrog directly to optimality, to build the foundations of optimal AI today. But in general, you know, our vision is very ambitious and I'm not saying that we're going to be successful. We have maybe a 10 or 15 percent chance of success. But that is enough that it's worth trying, right? I think in general among listeners, if you have a big idea and it has a very low chance of success, but if it works it's going to be big and no one else is going to be working on it—it's not something popular. If you don't do it, no one else will do it. And this is basically our situation. If you're in this situation, then you should try your chance and you should go and work on it. I mean, that's almost like the mission statement of Y Combinator, the thing that you just said. Yeah, the reason it's important is that again, if we don't do it no one else will do it, right? So it's worth trying. Even if we don't succeed, it's worth trying. Has the success very specifically of the coding agents built on top of the LLM stack surprised you at all? Like say over the last six months or so? Yeah, absolutely. I think it has surprised many people and it definitely did surprise me. If you look at why everything is starting to work so well with coding agents, it's really because code provides you with a verifiable reward signal. I think right now we're in this situation where any problem where the solutions you propose can be formally verified and you can actually trust a reward signal—it's not just some guess made by a model—any domain like this can be fully automated with current technology. Code is sort of like the first domain to fall, but there will be many others in the future. I think mathematics is also primed to see a revolution in the next few years for the same reasons again, because the domain just gives you verifiable rewards. I guess the challenge for a formally verified domain is you have to somehow take a domain and make it verifiable. That is the trick. I mean, code is very natural; you can test for bugs, it compiles, etc. Mathematics as well, where all the theorems and proofs work out. I guess it becomes more nebulous when you go a couple degrees off where there are fields that are not naturally formally verified. You need to come up with some sort of a function to come up with that reward that makes it verifiable. How do you do that with very fuzzy things like the English language and composing the perfect essay? How do you make that formally verifiable? Yeah, absolutely. I mean writing essays is the typical example of a domain that's not verifiable. And so what you're going to see is that the progress of reasoning models and base LLMs on this type of domain is going to be very slow. The stack we're using, like the LLM stack, is very, very reliant on its training data. It's basically just operationalizing the training data. And for writing essays, the training data is coming from human experts annotating answers. And that's costly. So you're going to see this very, very slow progress; maybe it's even going to stall. But for any verifiable domain, take code for instance, the big unlock was when people started creating these code-based training environments for post-training where the reward signal is provided by things like unit tests and so on. That means that the model was not just working from human-provided annotations. It was actually trying its own things, verifying the answer, and generating a lot more training data in the process. It was a much denser coverage of the problem space. It wasn't just coverage in terms of, "Is the answer right or wrong?" but also starting to build models of the execution traces. The models could start incorporating an execution model, very much the way that human programmers do when they look at code. They're sort of executing the code in their minds and they keep track of the value variables. This is also what the models are trying to do now and this is why it's working so well. It's possible because you're working with this very formal, fully verifiable environment. You cannot do that with essays. You cannot do that with law or many other problems. I think I really like how you define intelligence and how to measure it, which brings to the question of sharing the history of ARC-AGI. Yeah, so my definition of general intelligence—many people around the industry these days say AGI is going to be a system that can automate most economically valuable tasks. To me, that definition is about automation. It's not about intelligence; it's not about general intelligence. So my definition is that AGI is basically going to be a system that can approach any new problem and any new task and make sense of it. It should model it and become competent at it with the same degree of efficiency as a human could. Meaning it's going to need basically the same amount of training data and training compute as a human, which is very little. Humans are really, really data efficient. General intelligence is human-level skill acquisition efficiency on the same scope of tasks that humans could potentially learn to do. Do you think it's possible that we will accomplish the first definition of AGI—the automate most economically useful work—before we accomplish your definition? Absolutely, I think that's the trajectory that we're on right now. I think it's already true that in principle, current technology can fully automate to human-level or beyond any domain where you have verifiable rewards. Code being the first one. And I think figuring out AGI, figuring out human-level learning efficiency over arbitrary tasks, that's probably going to take a different sort of technology, a different mindset, and a different approach. Do you think that LLMs can be bent to have the same sample efficiency as humans, or do you think it's fundamentally just impossible and we need a new approach? Is that the thing that you're hoping to solve? With enough compute, everything starts looking like everything else. Compute is a great equalizer; every approach starts looking the same. I think it's possible in principle to build something that looks a lot like AGI on top of the LLM stack, but it's not going to be LLMs per se. It's going to be this new layer. Perhaps it's going to be even a few layers above. But you can build it on top of LLMs because LLMs are a kind of computer, right? I see. For this reason, future AI in a few decades is not going to be this harness on top of a reasoning model on top of a base LLM. It's going to be much, much lower than that. To Diana's question, do you want to talk about how you actually designed ARC-AGI and why it's a good barometer of that? I mean, I've been doing deep learning for a very, very long time. Initially my take, my mindset, was that deep learning was going to be able to do everything. You were the creator of Keras before even all the other frameworks became very popular. That's right, that's right. I was training deep learning models for natural language processing in fact in 2014. From that work, I actually started developing this open-source library which I released exactly 11 years ago, March 2015. So that was Keras, and then it got popular and then I ended up doing less of the research that I had started Keras for and more working on the framework itself just because it had really good product-market fit. My take around that time, 2015-2016, was that deep learning was extremely general and that you could do everything with deep learning. You didn't need anything else. It was Turing complete, so my take was basically that deep learning was differentiable programming. Anything you would do with software, you could in principle train a deep learning model on the right inputs and outputs to do the same thing. In 2016, I was doing research at Google Brain on trying to train deep learning models to help with reasoning problems, in particular first-order logic problems and theorem proving. I started finding that you could not really get gradient descent to encode reasoning-style algorithms. It was not because the models could not represent these algorithms; it was because gradient descent could not find them. So the problem was that it wasn't about deep learning not being Turing complete; that was not the problem. The problem was gradient descent, right. Gradient descent would not find generalizable programs. It would instead end up doing overfit pattern matching over sequences of input tokens. Which I guess people could argue is what's happening today. I mean, that's still what's happening today in a slightly higher-level version of that. It's with a lot of data, so it doesn't feel like overfitting because the data has a lot more distribution. Yeah, with a lot more data, and also I think models today are a lot more compressive of the data, which is why they generalize better. So all models are wrong, but some models are useful. And then I guess what I'm hearing is like your method might find the right model. That's right. That's where the idea came from. At the time back in 2016 or 2017, I was like, "Okay, we're going to need a benchmark to capture these ideas. We're going to need a program synthesis benchmark." And my mental model for that was ImageNet. I was like, "Oh, I'm going to make the ImageNet of reasoning." So I started brainstorming a few ideas around 2017 and explored many different things. I tried working with in particular cellular automata—a setup where you show a model cellular automata outputs and it must recreate the program that generated them. Eventually I settled on the ARC-AGI format around early 2018. I was doing this on the side; it was a side project. My main project was developing Keras at Google, so I wasn't moving very fast on that. In summer 2018, I wrote the ARC task editor and then I started just making lots of tasks by hand. About one year later, I had made 1,000 tasks. I wrote up the paper that was explaining what this was about and what the big idea was, like intelligence as skill acquisition efficiency, and I published all of that in 2019. In parallel, GPT-3 2020 was coming out and starting to show signs. Until the ChatGPT moment around 2022 end of the year, the industry took off with that. And this was one of the benchmarks that was performing really badly. It was very obscure; I don't think many people knew about it. It was mostly niche research communities that maybe read your paper. Yeah, people who worked on program synthesis knew about it, but a lot of people who worked on deep learning on scaling up LLMs didn't really care for it. Part of the reason why is because LLMs did not work well, or at all, on the benchmark. For a benchmark to capture the attention of the research community, it needs to start working a little, right? If it's too hard, people are just going to dismiss it. You're just ahead of your time clearly because we're not on ARC-AGI 1 anymore. 2 is reaching saturation and then 3 is out now. Yes. And I think the cool thing about ARC-AGI is it has been a very good barometer for the industry with the big changes that happen. V1 was not working at all for a long time until 2025 when reasoning models came out, right? Yeah, absolutely. If you look at frontier AI performance on ARC V1 first and then V2, base LLMs were scoring extremely low on V1, basically sub 10 percent. That was true of the original GPT-3, which was scoring zero. But that's even true of the latest base LLMs today as of March 2026. Without reasoning. Without reasoning, yeah. So the base models' performance on V1 stayed very, very low, even though in the meantime we had scaled up these models by 50,000x. It was really telling you that more scale, scaling up pre-training alone, was not going to crack the benchmark. This was not enough to demonstrate that the model had fluid intelligence. The moment models started performing well on ARC V1 was with the first reasoning models. So in particular the OpenAI O1 and then O3 models which, by the way, were demonstrated by OpenAI on ARC because it was the one unsaturated reasoning benchmark that was really showing that this model was different. It had new capabilities that we had not seen before. With reasoning models, you start seeing this sudden step function change on ARC V1. ARC V1 was really the benchmark that signaled that at this moment in time, something was happening. Something big. Yes, something big—new capabilities were emerging like reasoning was new and different. And it was actually not obvious at the time. I don't know if you remember when O3 preview was announced by OpenAI. That was end of 2024 actually. Yeah, December 2024, and like, sure, it was like a huge step function progress on ARC, but it was very expensive. It did not really have product-market fit effectively. But if you looked at ARC results, you knew that this was big and important. And then we released ARC V2, which was the same format but more difficult, with more composition at the level of the reasoning chains. And what happened is that the earliest reasoning models started very, very low on ARC V2. And then around the same time as coding agents started working—this year. Yeah, very, very recently, just a few months ago, you saw this very, very fast saturation of ARC V2. Again, V2 signaled that yes, there was this new set of capabilities emerging like agentic coding. So I think the benchmark did a really good job at capturing the advent of reasoning models and then the advent of agentic coding. It's this new paradigm where if you have verifiable rewards, then you can basically fully automate the domain, which by the way is true of ARC. ARC does provide a verifiable reward. I guess for V2, what caused the saturation? V1 was clearly reasoning. V2—a benchmark doesn't care how you solve it. Were people using codegen to then solve V2? That's right. So not necessarily codegen per se, but the frontier labs had been targeting ARC V2 and the progress you saw is actually a result of this very, very large-scale targeting. What you can do to solve ARC V2 is you ask your reasoning model to make more tasks like those in the benchmark. Then you try to solve them using program induction, for instance, still using your reasoning model. Then you verify the solution. Again, it's verifiable so you can trust the answer. Then you fine-tune the model on the successful reasoning chains. And you can keep repeating—you generate new tasks, you solve them, you verify the solution, and you fine-tune the model on the reasoning chains. You can keep doing this millions of times. You just need to spend more money. This is the RL loop that is happening. This is exactly what's happening, yeah. And the new paradigm in AI is basically that any domain where this is true, where you have the ability to generate these true verification signals, you can run this kind of loop. And if you can run this kind of loop, you can brute-force mine effectively the entire space and get extremely high performance. This is basically the process through which ARC V2 was saturated. What it tells you is that it's not so much that the models have higher fluid intelligence than they did with the first reasoning models. It's just that you have this new paradigm of post-training. This is exactly what led to agentic coding, so it does matter; it is valuable and useful. It's not that the models are smarter; it's that they're suddenly more useful. And it is possible to be more useful in particular domains without being smarter. Yeah, absolutely, because when it comes to competency, there's always a tradeoff between intelligence and knowledge. If you have more knowledge, if you have better training, you need less intelligence to be competent. That's exactly what happened with the rise of coding agents. The models don't have higher fluid intelligence per se. They don't have a higher IQ, so to speak. It's just that they're way better trained. They're way better trained in two ways: they're not just trying to autocomplete code anymore; they're actually trained via trial and error in these RL post-training environments with true reward signals. Also, they're trained to embed this model of code execution where they learn to keep track of the value variables over an execution cycle. That's what is leading to this extremely strong product-market fit of agentic coding today and it's completely changing software engineering. This happened not too long ago, the saturation. We actually had the founder of Poetic that came and spoke about their approach, which really sounds like this new way of getting LLMs to perform—building this agent harness. The harness is basically structuring a problem domain into something that can be formally verified. They did that for ARC V2 which, when they released it, they were at the top of the benchmark. But then the crazy thing is I actually work with a company in the Winter 26 batch called Confluence Labs which actually ended up saturating the V2 results with 97 percent. I think their task cost was a lot more efficient too. That approach they took is similar to this. They built the harnesses on top of it in order to get the LLMs to go and build different tasks and program through it. Which then for me I was like, "Wow, it's this batch." During the batch they only worked on it for a couple of months and they were able to saturate this benchmark that has been around for a long time. It's like something special is happening. Yeah, yeah, there's a lot of progress right now that's driven by custom harnesses around the task. The harness is basically a way for the human programmer to input into the model higher-level solution strategies, basically. To me, the fact that you need humans to engineer these harnesses is also a sign that we're short of AGI today. If we had AGI, AGI would just make its own harness. It would not need to be told how to solve a problem; it would just figure it out. But it is very effective. I don't think harnesses get us closer to AGI in any sense, but it's a very valuable area of research because that can lead to task automation at scale. YC's next batch is now taking applications. Got a startup in you? Apply at ycombinator.com/apply. It's never too early and filling out the app will level up your idea. Okay, back to the video. Can you tell us about then what V3 is going to measure that's just got released? Yeah, absolutely. If you look at V1 and V2, it was really focusing on your ability to produce causal models of a pattern that was just given to you. The data was given to you. So it was static, it was passive, and really focused on modeling. V3 is completely different. We're trying to measure agentic intelligence. It's interactive and it's active. The data is not provided to you; you must go get it. The idea is that your agent is dropped into a new environment which is kind of like a mini video game. It's not provided any instructions, it's not told what to do, it's not told what the goal even is or what the controls even are. It must figure out everything on its own via trial and error. So we're not just measuring the AI's ability to model its environment; we're also looking at its exploration efficiency, its ability to acquire goals on its own like goal-setting, and of course its ability to plan through the model of the environment it's created and to execute the plan. Together, all of these abilities we call agentic intelligence and we're looking for AI systems that could learn to play these games and crack them with the same degree of action efficiency as a human. If you look at a human, they're dropped into this new environment and they try a few things. They start understanding how things work. They can solve the environment in a few hundreds to thousands of actions. We're trying to look for AI systems that could match this efficiency. By the way, we know that all of these test environments in ARC-3 are solvable by humans with no prior training because we actually tested them on regular people. At first you just see this screen and you have these keys available but you don't know what they do. You must figure out everything from scratch. Humans are really good at that, by the way. They're really good at exploring efficiently, at making sense of something new, and eventually cracking the game. Frontier models today are not very good at it. If the reasoning models cracked V1 and the RL environments cracked V2, do we need a new advance to crack V3? Do even the best techniques currently not work? I mean, I'm pretty curious to see how frontier labs are going to react to V3 and how they're going to start to target it. It is designed to be more resistant to the same kind of targeting strategy as what we saw for V2. In particular, of course, you can try to just make more ARC-3-like games and then train your agents in them. The thing is, we've deliberately tried to create a private set of environments that is significantly different from the public set. You can look at the public set, but it's not actually giving you that much information about what's in the private set. In the private set you will have very different games with very different concepts. Also, the public set is meant to be substantially easier. So your performance on the public set is not actually representative of how well the system would do on the private set. For this reason, it's going to be harder to target. And that makes it a better test of fluid intelligence as opposed to a test of how much effort you put into cracking it. I'm so curious, how do you come up with these games? They're so creative. Yeah, we set up an entire video game studio right to create them. So we got over 250 games. They're pretty quick to play—each game takes you maybe 10 minutes or a bit less to play from scratch upon first contact. We have 250 plus. We set up this very productive game studio where any given week we had multiple games in progress. We had this pipeline including design, implementation, review, human testing, and many, many iteration cycles to make sure the game comes out right. Who's working in the studio? Who are the creators? Right, we hired a team of game developers and we built our own game engine. Wow, so it's actually people who previously worked in the video game industry. That's right. So one thing to keep in mind though is that the games in ARC-3 are unique. They're trying to not borrow elements or concepts from previous video games. They're built entirely on top of core knowledge priors. It is elementary knowledge like basic physics, understanding of objects, and understanding of the notion of agent. For instance, an agent is an object with goals and intentions. But we are not incorporating any language, any cultural symbols like arrows, or the color green meaning go and color red meaning stop. There's no external knowledge that's involved in these games. It's like one of those IQ tests that are just pattern matching but now it has time series. Yeah, and it's not just time series—it's interactive. You must create your own path through game space. You must gather the data and you must do so efficiently. Of course you could say, "Well, I'm just going to brute-force mine the space of every possible game state and then I'll find the solution." You cannot do that because if you try to do that, you would score extremely low even if you manage to solve the level. You're scored on your efficiency. You must match human-level efficiency. It's funny, it's like almost coming full circle. This level of AGI with games sort of is the match pair to OpenAI writing. Tom Brown, one of the co-founders of Anthropic, had to write the harness code to allow pre-GPT AI at OpenAI to play StarCraft. OpenAI worked on Dota 2 in particular. They had the OpenAI Five model which was mostly pre-transformers because they were working with a stack of LSTM layers if I recall correctly. Even before OpenAI, DeepMind worked a lot on video games via deep RL and they were the first to do Atari games back in 2013. They were very early and very visionary in that sense. The big difference is that if you look at Atari games for instance, or even Dota, you're training on the same environment as what you use for testing. Effectively, you're just trying to memorize the best strategies. You're trying to explore the full space of possible game states and operationalize that knowledge. Then at inference time, you're basically just recalling that knowledge. That's explicitly what we're trying to avoid with ARC-3. You're not playing games that you've seen before. You're not playing games that you've been trained on for millions of hours. The OpenAI Five model was playing a restricted version of Dota 2 and it was trained on an insane amount of training data. With ARC-3, you're being evaluated on games that you're seeing for the very first time and every action you spend exploring is counted towards your efficiency score. You're really focused on measuring fluid intelligence—your ability to efficiently explore, efficiently produce a world model of the environment, and then use this model to infer goals, plan, and eventually crack the game. One of the arguments for Ndea is that you're able to do all of the intelligent tasks for a fraction of the cost. An ARC task might be .3 cents, but for the same task on a foundation model with LLMs it's a dollar to ten dollars. There's this other aspect where it seems like more and more intelligence on the LLM side can be distilled down into smaller and smaller models. On the one hand they're scaling up, but then they're distilling smarter small models. I guess your approach might indicate that achieving AGI might not be inherently a scale thing at all. There's a platonic ideal of the Ndea model that achieves AGI. Do you ever think about it in terms of like, well, it would fit on a floppy disk? Well okay, there are two things to separate. There's the sort of fluid intelligence engine. I think it's going to be a very, very small codebase and a very small set of models associated with it. It's probably going to be on the order of megabytes. And then you have the knowledge base that's going to be layered below this fluid intelligence engine. Fluid intelligence has to draw on some knowledge and that knowledge is going to take up a lot more space. So I think it's important to differentiate the two. I do believe that when we create AGI, retrospectively it will turn out that it's a codebase that's less than 10,000 lines of code. And if you had known about it back in the 1980s, you could have done AGI back then using the compute resources available back then. Wow, that's a crazy prediction. I think retrospectively this will turn out to be true. Wow, so it was just hiding under our noses in plain sight for 40 years. It took us 40 years to figure it out. That's right, that's right. Well that second thing sounds like Douglas Lenat's Cyc project, or is that the wrong way to think about it? Knowledge about the world and then there are methods like the program. What I hear is the program might be 10,000 lines and then it operates on a knowledge base that's very large. The problem with Cyc was that there was no learning involved. It was just handcrafted knowledge; it was purely symbolic knowledge and it was probably inaccurate. The way you want to be building AGI is that you want to be removing humans from the improvement loop as much as possible. You don't want a system where every improvement in system capability has to involve a human engineer doing something. It's actually the strength of deep learning and foundation models—you can just scale up the knowledge base. An LLM is effectively a knowledge base. You can scale up that knowledge base by just adding training data and training compute with no further human involvement. I mean of course there's still a little bit of human involvement in making sure the training job completes, but it's minor. You've managed to remove humans from this improvement loop as much as possible. And that's also what we want for our system. We want a system that's self-improving where the improvements are compounding, meaning that every time the system increases its capabilities, it's also increasing the rate at which it increases its capabilities. I think this is a PG-ism: "I'm sorry the essay is so long, if I had more time I would make it shorter." Yeah, when you're looking at a hard problem, it's actually harder to produce a short, elegant, concise solution than a messy, over-engineered solution. You can brute-force it, but the more elegant version is very, very short and concise. And that's kind of like what you said with how this might come about. I think this is literally the shape of the type of AI approach we're creating. I think this is also the shape of science itself. Science is fundamentally a symbolic compression process where you're looking at a big mess of observations and you're compressing that down to a very simple symbolic rule. You're saying, "Yeah, like all these thousands of observations actually just follow this one simple equation." That is symbolic compression, and to do this by the way you need the model to be symbolic. You could not fit a curve and say, "Well you know, that curve is my model." That would never be optimal; that would never be concise or elegant enough. That's not what science is doing. Science is not about curve fitting; science is about finding the equation. It is about finding the most compressive symbolic model of your pile of observations. And that's the process that we're trying to recreate in software form. You could say that the Ndea approach to program synthesis is that we're building science incarnate—the scientific method in algorithmic form. I'm curious if you compare it to biology. Clearly LLMs don't learn the way that humans do because no baby reads the whole internet. Do you think program synthesis is closer to the way that humans learn, or do you think that's yet a third branch? I do think humans do some amount of program synthesis. I think the way humans learn and the way the human mind works is very messy. It's not like there's one simple elegant principle behind it all. It's an implementation of fundamental principles of intelligence. I think we can identify these principles and re-implement intelligence from scratch from first principles in a way that will be much more efficient than the human brain. I think the human brain is messy and it can be a good source of inspiration for AI, but I think it would be counterproductive to just try to observe it and re-implement it like and make it biologically plausible. That's not what we're trying to do at Ndea. We're really trying to find what are the first principles of intelligence and what is the system that would best implement them. But yeah, I do believe the human mind does at the highest level something that looks a lot like program synthesis. Like we're currently building causal models of our surroundings. We're describing our surroundings in our mind as a set of objects and agents and relations between objects that are fundamentally symbolic and causal in nature. This is exactly the process that lets us generalize so well and adapt so well to novelty on the fly. I'm curious about Ndea the company as you're building it. We've all here heard the OpenAI founding story and something that always struck me was just like both Sam and Greg say that it was a little odd in the early days because they didn't actually know what to do. They sort of just had a bunch of people hang out in an apartment. I would love to hear kind of what that's been like for Ndea. What did day one look like? And just maybe for people who are interested in starting these alternative approaches who don't have a researchy background, how should they think about that? Yeah, so we started on day one with the symbolic learning vision. We basically knew that we wanted to do symbolic program synthesis, that we wanted to create a new approach to machine learning where you replace parametric curves with the shortest possible symbolic models. And then the big question was okay, so how do we find these models? We started from the base idea, which is still the idea that we're following today, which is that we are going to do deep learning-guided program search. You have a symbolic search space to explore and it's big—it's in fact combinatorial. You're not going to make progress if you just use brute-force. It's not going to scale. You have to break the combinatorial wall and the way to do it is to add deep learning guidance. It's actually very similar to the principles that underlie something like AlphaGo or AlphaZero. That was our starting point. We also didn't have very clear ideas about how to build it, so we tried many different things. It took us half a year roughly to get to good foundations where we could start building a system that compounds. I think that's what's really important when doing a lab like this: you don't want to be in a situation where you're constantly trying something new and it's not reusing any learnings from previous approaches. You want a compounding stack. You want to build reusable foundations and then a next layer and then a next layer. And of course you want to be building on top of the right foundations so don't commit to the foundation layer too early, but also make sure that at some point you're building this compounding structure. And that's the situation that we're in now. Is ARC-3 the end or will there be an ARC 4, 5, 6? Can you keep making it harder? Yeah, yeah, I think there will absolutely be ARC-4 and ARC-5. I mean, we're currently planning ARC-5. The point of the ARC-AGI benchmark series is not to say that, "Well, you know, here's this test; if you pass it this is AGI." Instead what we're trying to do is we target the residual gap of AI capabilities. Frontier AI is advancing and we're saying, "Well, if you compare it to human ability, there's all these tasks, all these things it's not doing well." So we're going to create a benchmark to target that. And so it's a moving target, right? It's not a fixed point. There will be ARC-4 which will be in the spirit of ARC-3 but more focused on continual learning and curriculum learning at longer time scales. You're going to have fewer games but they're going to have way more levels and the levels are going to be compounding, meaning that for each level you need to reuse stuff that you've learned before. And then there's going to be ARC-5. I'm actually really, really excited about ARC-5; it's very, very new and different and it's all about invention. And I mean, you will see what that means. Eventually I expect we will run out of things to test. As we get closer to AGI, eventually there will be no measurable difference between human capabilities and frontier AI. When that happens, when it becomes effectively impossible to measure the gap, this is the AGI moment. Well then the machines will take over and then they will create ARC-ASI-1. Yes, ARC-ASI. And then it will continue from there. Exciting. Yeah, if you had to put a guess, I mean—years, decades, months? My timeline to AGI, you know if you just try to extrapolate from the current rate of progress and the amount of investment that's going into not just the LLM stack but also side ideas and side bets that might work out—like Ndea for instance—I think we're probably looking at AGI early 2030s most likely. Around the time that we're going to be releasing like maybe ARC-6 or ARC-7, that's probably going to be AGI. You guys are doing a different approach to LLMs. Do you think there's room for more startups to explore other new approaches and are there any others that you think are promising but don't have time to explore yourself? Yeah, absolutely. I mean there are many different approaches that you could try. I've said compute is a great equalizer. If you look at the amount of compute and resources that we've thrown at deep learning and gradient descent and scaling that up, if we had thrown the same amount of investment into almost anything else, you would also have seen extremely exciting results. Take genetic algorithms for instance. If you try to scale up genetic algorithms, I mean, I'm sure you can do incredible things with that. You could in fact probably do new science because that's based on search, and search is the best fit for automating the scientific method. Right now there's also approaches that build on top of the current stack but they're slightly alternative, like state space models. There's the XLSTM architecture. You can basically—current frontier AI is a stack of things and you can take any layer in the stack and try to propose an alternative. If you propose an alternative architecture you can be doing, for instance, more like recurrent models instead of transformers for the architecture. Or you can do even lower level. You're going to be like, "Okay, we're still going to be training parametric curves but we're going to get rid of gradient descent. We're going to use search. Maybe you're going to do neuroevolution." That's lower level. And the lowest level is the level where we're operating, where we're saying, "Well actually, forget about curves. Forget about parametric learning. Forget about gradient descent. We're just going to do something completely different." I think if you want to build optimal AI, you're kind of forced to go back to the foundation of the stack. It cannot be like one layer added on top of the pile. So do you think for aspiring researchers who want to do a new neo-lab with a different approach, they should be reading research papers from the 70s or 80s and go deeply in those? That is actually a great idea because earlier in the history of the AI research timeline, people were exploring more things and very different things. You've had this sort of collapse of everything into one approach. It's actually kind of a bad idea. Consider that not too long ago, about 20 years ago—we had the collapse into SVMs too. Yeah, I mean I wouldn't describe it as a collapse because there weren't that many people doing SVMs and AI was a much, much smaller field back then. But there was this widespread understanding that neural networks were a failed approach. Neural networks didn't work and it was a waste of time to keep trying them. Even in the 90s, right? No, even in the late 2000s this was still the thing. Basically, when I got into AI people were telling me like, "Hey, neural networks—don't try that." I was like, "Yeah, but it looks a lot like what the brain is doing." If everybody is working on the same thing, you are discarding ideas that will actually turn out to be very productive ideas, right? And yeah, back in the 70s and 80s people were trying more things and I think genetic algorithms are actually a very good example of that. I think this is an approach that has a tremendous amount of potential, but not too many people are looking into scaling it up deeply. Are there any characteristics that you would be looking for? Is it as simple as if there's a scaling law that could happen then even if it's different, or is that too like thinking by analogy? I think you are looking for approaches that scale. I think it's a non-starter if you're working on something but the only way to increase the capabilities of the system is to have human engineers and researchers spend time on it. It will not work because even if the idea is very clever and very elegant and works really well, capabilities are going to be bounded. They're going to be bounded by human investment, right? You want to be in a setup where the system can improve its capabilities with no human in the loop, with no human bottleneck. So you would say, like, don't just do it the way we did it 10 years ago—do it with the idea that recursive self-improvement is baked in at the beginning. Yeah, not necessarily recursive self-improvement, because deep learning for instance is not recursively self-improving, but with the idea of scaling up with no human bottleneck. You want to remove the human from the improvement loop. The great strength of deep learning is that the models got better and better simply by adding training compute and training data. I mean it's a little bit of a caricature because, of course, just adding these factors requires a lot of human involvement, but basically that's the idea. It is that you have this decoupling from the improvement curve and the amount of human effort that's needed to be injected into the system. I guess, or human effort that's already happened, because the LLMs do actually require an enormous amount of human effort; it's just it was the human effort to build the internet and we'd already built it. Yeah, actually less and less now that we're doing training in interactive verifiable environments, because then you only need a small amount of human effort to create the environment. From that small amount of effort, you're creating exponentially more training data. But at first, I think to sort of like prime the machine, you need this tremendous amount of human-generated abstractions encoded in text data. And if you don't start from that, you cannot get the system into this loop. Do you have any advice for me starting an open-source project? Things to do, things not to do in the AI space, because I am not sure how I signed up for this in the last 14 days, but I think I have, I don't know, on the order of like 10 to 30,000 people using G-stack every day. So right now I'm less involved with it; there's a big team at Google that's working on it and they're doing an amazing job. So it is possible to not to, you know, to put people together to like keep it going. It is possible to start something, it is possible to start something and then get more people involved, and at some point it becomes its own thing. It used to be your baby but now it's all grown up, it's all adult and going on with its own life. So if you ask me the factors that really made Keras successful, I mean, first of all is that there was this big focus on making the API simple and intuitive; there was this big focus on usability. And this was inspired by scikit-learn. Like scikit-learn was sort of like the OG machine learning library for Python, and what made it successful was that it was so easy to get started with it. So at first I was like, okay, I'm going to package all this functionality I've created under really, really simple APIs; it's going to be like the scikit-learn API, that was like the big idea. The focus on usability is not just making sure the API is simple, it's also making sure the entire onboarding experience is nice and easy. Like the docs should be very informative. You should, you know, the docs should be not just telling you about how to use this thing, but they should actually be teaching you about the domain in the first place, because the folks who land on your website they're not going to be already deep learning experts. They're going to be people looking to maybe start using deep learning, and so you have to teach them not just how to use the tool, but what the tool is good for and the entire field around it. And then you know, you have to put a lot of investment into community building. One thing we did a bit at Google—in fact, you know, Google made it kind of difficult and I was sad about that—is hire your power users. Like, hire your fans; this is a really, really good idea. Like, find the most enthusiastic users from your community and just hire them on your team. Amazing. Yeah, and these are always the best people, right? All right, time to start gstack.org, put in a bunch of my own money and then hire a bunch of people to work on it, that sounds good. Like, what would you tell them? If you were 18 right now, what would you tell them? Yeah, I mean, there's a lot of people today who are very pessimistic, very negative takes about the rise in AI capabilities. And yeah, more people should have this mindset of trying to learn as much as possible, not just about AI, but about the domain that they want to apply AI to.
