Trump Stacked Tech Council With ‘Star Power,’ David Sacks Says

Bloomberg Technology(719.0K) ·10:33 ·610 views · 2026-03-27
David Sacks (Partner, Craft Ventures & Co-chair, PCAST) Ed Ludlow (Host, Bloomberg Technology)
President Trump has revitalized the President’s Council of Advisors on Science and Technology (PCAST) with a "star power" roster of industry leaders to establish a unified federal AI framework and ensure American technological dominance through a "one rulebook" regulatory approach.
TL;DR President Trump has revitalized the President’s Council of Advisors on Science and Technology (PCAST) with a "star power" roster of industry leaders to establish a unified federal AI framework and ensure American technological dominance through a "one rulebook" regulatory...

TL;DR

President Trump has revitalized the President’s Council of Advisors on Science and Technology (PCAST) with a "star power" roster of industry leaders to establish a unified federal AI framework and ensure American technological dominance through a "one rulebook" regulatory approach.

The New PCAST: Composition and Mandate

  • Advisory Role: PCAST is a federal advisory committee, originally established in its current form in 1990, designed to provide high-quality evidence and recommendations to the White House on science and technology policy.
  • "Builder" Focused Membership: Co-chaired by David Sacks and Michael Kratsios, the council currently features 15 members (with a capacity for 24), including CEOs Jensen Huang (Nvidia), Lisa Su (AMD), Mark Zuckerberg (Meta), and Michael Dell.
      • Applied Experience: Sacks emphasized that the council prioritizes "builders and doers" who have created companies, rather than just academic researchers.
      • Technological Breadth: Expertise spans software, advanced semiconductors, quantum computing, fusion, and small modular reactors.
  • Sacks’ Role: Moving from a temporary 130-day Special Government Employee (SGE) role into a permanent co-chair position, Sacks will oversee recommendations on AI and an expanded range of technology topics.

The "One Rulebook" National AI Framework

  • Regulatory Uniformity: A primary goal is to replace the "patchwork" of 50 different state regulations with a single national framework to simplify compliance for innovators.
  • Legislative Pillars: The administration’s AI policy is built on four pillars: pro-innovation, pro-infrastructure, pro-energy, and pro-export.
  • Key Policy Provisions:
      • Child Safety: Addressing online protections for minors.
      • Content Creation: Developing principles to protect content creators while allowing for the training of AI models.
      • Bipartisan Outlook: Sacks expressed optimism that a bipartisan framework could pass Congress within the next few months, noting that even Democratic criticism has been "muted."

Infrastructure and Energy Policy

  • Ratepayer Protection Pledge: The framework includes provisions to ensure that new AI data centers do not increase electricity costs for residential consumers.
  • Energy Self-Sufficiency: The administration seeks to make it easier for AI companies to "bring their own power" to facilities.
  • Contrast with Critics: Sacks positioned this approach as a pro-growth alternative to proposed bans or heavy restrictions on new data centers suggested by some members of Congress.

Global Competition and Export Strategy

  • Market Share as Security: The administration maintains a "pro-export mindset," viewing global market share as the primary way for the American tech stack to become the global standard.
  • Strategic Nuance: While seeking global dominance, Sacks noted that exports to "countries of concern" (e.g., China) require a nuanced policy to protect national security.
  • The Internet Model: The goal is to replicate the global success of the American-led internet within the AI and semiconductor sectors.

This is an area where I think we're we're willing and and happy to work with Democrats and I think there's a lot of Democrats who would like to see a single national framework as well. Anne Marte Pensgaard: Why does Norway dramatically outperform at the Olympics? | In Good Company

Norges Bank Investment Management(104.0K) ·31:00 ·1.8K views · 2026-03-27
Anne Marte Pensgaard (Professor of Sports Psychology, Olympiatoppen) Nicolai Tangen (CEO, Norges Bank Investment Management)
Norway’s global sports dominance is rooted in a "long-game" strategy that prioritizes social cohesion and multi-sport play over early specialization, paired with advanced mental training focused on psychological safety and mindfulness.
TL;DR Norway’s global sports dominance is rooted in a "long-game" strategy that prioritizes social cohesion and multi-sport play over early specialization, paired with advanced mental training focused on psychological safety and mindfulness. The Under-12 Rule: In Norway,...

TL;DR

Norway’s global sports dominance is rooted in a "long-game" strategy that prioritizes social cohesion and multi-sport play over early specialization, paired with advanced mental training focused on psychological safety and mindfulness.

The "Norwegian Model" of Youth Development

  • The Under-12 Rule: In Norway, youth sports emphasize play and community over competition; result lists and individual prizes are prohibited until age 12.
      • Retention over Selection: By not "singling out" talent early, Norway keeps a larger pool of youth active for longer, ensuring a broader talent base to draw from in later years.
  • Anti-Specialization: Pensgaard advocates for children playing multiple sports to develop physical flexibility, diverse mindsets, and social adaptability.
      • Late Blooming: Many of Norway's top athletes only specialized at ages 15–17. Early pressure often leads to burnout or injury before peak potential is reached.
  • Knowledge Transfer: The system encourages "meeting places" like Olympiatoppen (the national elite sports center), where athletes from different disciplines—from skiing to football—collaborate and share training insights.

Mental Edge: Attention, Values, and Gratitude

  • Attention Training: Elite performance relies on a "flexible focus system." Athletes are trained to stay in the moment rather than being paralyzed by pressure or low confidence.
  • Cognitive Diffusion: A core psychological technique is teaching athletes to distance themselves from their internal dialogue.
      • The "Not My Thoughts" Concept: Athletes learn to say, "I have a thought that I am not confident," rather than "I am not confident." This separation allows them to execute tasks regardless of their current feelings.
  • The Power of Gratitude: Pensgaard incorporates gratitude into elite training to ground athletes and improve brain function.
      • Perspective: Reminding athletes to be grateful for their environment (e.g., training in the mountains) builds humbleness, which balances the high-intensity courage required for competition.
  • Values-Based Motivation: Training starts with the "Why." Pensgaard identifies whether an athlete’s drive is aligned with their goals and if they are willing to embody values like bravery rather than taking shortcuts.

Building High-Performing Teams

  • Psychological Safety: Pensgaard defines this not as "job security for losers," but as the freedom to disagree or critique without fear of punishment or loss of status.
      • Open Communication: A culture dies when trust vanishes. Leaders must be secure enough to invite opinions from "outspoken athletes" or employees.
  • Cooperation Among Competitors: Norway’s downhill skiing team is a prime example of athletes who "beat the shit out of each other" in competition but train as a unified group.
      • Shared Success: The philosophy is that a successful team environment elevates every individual's performance level.
  • Simple Respect Rules: High-performing teams often rely on basic social norms to maintain cohesion, such as prohibiting phones during meals or sharing household chores during training camps.

Applications for Business and Leadership

  • Celebration as Fuel: Unlike the "dull" business world, sports embrace high-emotion celebrations. Pensgaard argues that business leaders should celebrate small wins more visibly to provide the inspiration needed to sustain hard work.
  • Broad Competence: Just as multi-sport athletes are more resilient, business professionals benefit from "diverse skill backgrounds," which allow them to analyze situations from multiple perspectives.
  • Managing Pressure: Adopting the mantra "Pressure is a privilege" helps reframe high-stakes environments as a sign of an interesting and meaningful career.

Notable Quotes

"We are a tiny country. We need to take care of everyone who wants to participate... When you are a child of 12, you know who’s the best one. You don’t need to have a prize for that person." — Anne Marte Pensgaard
"I’m not my thoughts. I can still do the task even though I think I feel not confident. Confidence then becomes a result of your focus, rather than a prerequisite for action." — Anne Marte Pensgaard
"A good culture can die in a second if the trust is not there anymore... You have to work on it, keep working on it, and be open for change all the time." — Anne Marte Pensgaard
"Pressure is a privilege. The fact that you have pressure means that you have an interesting job." — Nicolai Tangen
Hi, everyone. I'm Nicolai Tangen, the CEO of the Norwegian Sovereign Wealth Fund. And today we are doing something really great fun, because Norway happened to smash every record in the Winter Olympics. And today we are joined by Anne Marte Pensgaard, who is the professor of sports psychology, and is basically the person who's shaped the whole mental training of the Norwegian Olympic athletes. So, warm welcome. Thank you so much. Now, how do you explain all these medals? Oh, that's a really complex question. It goes a long way back, because we tend to think about what we do today. But of course, it has been a long tradition for maybe 20 or 30 years now, where we have worked on this culture. So I think there are many pieces in that puzzle. When did you decide to start? Because it was after Torino, I guess, or when did you think that, "Hey, we need to do some stuff here"? Well, it was even before that, actually. After the Calgary Games, we didn't take any medals. And then we started to think, "What should we do about this?" We had to do something about the whole top sport level system, and also work more together as teams, and think long term to develop athletes. Okay. So you are in Calgary. It's the worst games the country's ever had. So you sit down. And what kind of plan do you come up with? How do you do it? Fortunately, I was not there. So I started later. But Thor Ole Rimejorde was the one who kind of founded Olympiatoppen, which is our kind of top sport center where everybody meets and collaborates and shares ideas. And that was a really important starting point. And then we had some good leaders who really focused on expertise, I would say, in different areas: physiological, nutrition, psychology, and also more kind of training insights into the training regime, and also how to develop talents. So many things happened in the late '80s and early '90s. How long does it take from when you start a process like that until you really see the results? I think that's a good major point, is that this takes time. It's not a quick fix in any way. And of course, you need to have athletes who really want to be dedicated, who want to put in the hours to do this immense training. So you need to be patient. And also... but of course, you want to have results fast as well. So you kind of have to have two focuses at the same time: try to develop instant performances, but also think long term in order to sustain that kind of performance over time. There is something called the Norwegian model. What is that? Well, that's actually the way we treat our youth sport. And that's where we're not allowed to kind of have result lists or give only prizes to one person up to the age of 12. So if I get a prize, you get a prize, even though I might be faster than you. And that kind of also helps us think that these are children. They are supposed to have fun and play, not to be kind of singled out as talent at an early age. I think that is a key element in why we are able to have a lot of youth doing sports for a long time. And then we have more to pick from. And also we are a tiny country. We need to kind of take care of everyone who wants to participate. There is something very social-democratic about the fact that everybody gets the same prize, no? Yeah, it is. But, you know, when you are a child at 12, you know who's the best one. You don't need to have a prize for that person. And at the same time, they forget the results two minutes later. If you ask a kid what the result from the football pitch was, they might even not remember the score. So it's important to keep the perspective on this. They should develop on their own terms. And the most important thing is that they are social with their friends. So then they will stick into the sport. Do you think the kind of result lists and the awards is the reason why people drop off in sports, let's say in the US, much quicker? I think there's a lot more pressure on young kids in the US. We stayed there for some time, and also were able to see the system from that perspective, and it's very different. And I also think the Norwegian system allows a child to try different sports for a longer period of time instead of just choosing one. We're kind of maybe losing track of that at the moment. So I think we need to really focus on what we did 10 years ago, because that's the result of what we see today. That's why these athletes perform today. It's not what they do today necessarily, but what they have done for many, many years. It's interesting how some of the top talents were doing several sports, right? I mean, even the chess player, Magnus Carlsen, was a good footballer, right? What is the case for doing many different things? There are many, many advantages by doing that, because both physically you are more prepared to the different new techniques that will develop. You also get more ability to be more flexible in your mindset. You are in different milieux, so that you have a different coach maybe, in different settings. It teaches you how to behave in different settings and to relate to people. So there's... and it's a lot more fun to do different things. And you are with your friends, hopefully for most of the time. So there are so many advantages of doing this. So we shouldn't lose track of that. And we also know that just by growing, children gain more kind of physical fitness. So we don't have to put that much effort into that at that moment. What are the similarities to business when it comes to that? Doing different things or specializing early? I think it's important in any kind of profession to know different skills. It gives you kind of a knowledge background so that you understand your coworkers, and also you are more flexible and able to analyze a situation from different perspectives, I think. So a broad background, I think, is beneficial in many ways, also in business, I would say. Now, there are many sports where we don't do well, or where Norway doesn't do well, right? I mean figure skating, ice hockey, and so on. Why are we not doing well in those sports? We don't have enough ice hockey rinks, you know? We don't have enough ice. We have so few places to go on ice. So if we had decided to develop that, I'm quite sure we would also excel in that area. So what I mean is the accessibility to do your sport is, of course, key. It's easy for us to go out and do skiing. It's far more difficult to get ice time somewhere. Why doesn't Sweden replicate what Norway does? Well, I think... I mean, Sweden is also quite successful, I would say, and also in some of the really huge sports. So we shouldn't kind of boast of knowing what to do and teach other cultures what to do. I think they do pretty well. But I think we in Norway have the advantage of being very close together with the science, the applied part, the athletes, and the coaches. It's quite a tight community. A lot of knowledge translation to other sports. And I think that's quite unique for the Norwegian system, that we are able to create these meeting places where we actually can talk across sports every day. When you contrast with, let's say the US, what's the biggest difference? Well, this is one of the things my US colleagues envy us for, that we are able to create these meeting places. And we also have, you know, several times before an Olympics, we have all the athletes gathered together, also to bond, to get to know each other, so that they, when they go to the Olympics, they know each other well. And this is a concept that the US have adopted as well, and try to do, but they are huge. I mean, their squad is three times bigger than ours in the Olympics. So it's, of course, more difficult for them, being such a huge country and much more people. How important is it to have specific stars or role models which create these clusters? Again, I think that's a key thing. You need the role models. You need those who pave the way for other people. And when you see that, you think, "Oh, can you succeed? I can succeed." And that's super important. So let's say in alpine skiing, when Ole Kristian Furuseth kind of took a World Cup medal, he was... the other teammates were just looking at each other and said, "Well, if he can... I beat him every day on training. So if he can, I can." And that's kind of important in every place. We have Ingebrigtsen, we have role models in so many different areas. And also now in football with Haaland and Ødegaard. It, of course, creates a belief that we also can do well when it comes to different sports. Moving on to the mental edge. So let's say now I'm a decent athlete. What do you as a sports psychologist do with me? If you are a decent athlete and good enough to be part of the Olympiatoppen system and you have a scholarship in our place, I would mainly focus on why do you want to do this sport? Why did you become a skier, or why did you become a footballer, or what kind of was the drive behind your choice of sport? And why do you do it now? And what do you want to achieve? Well, I want to win. I want to show the world. Yeah, then we need to know what the potential is and how far you are off that kind of goal, to see what you need to do or work. And are you actually willing to put in that amount of work that you need to do? Because, oh, it's a huge job to be a champion. Huge job. Okay, so I want to be a champion. I'm going to put in whatever it takes. What do you do with my mind, with my brain? Then you need to learn how to focus. Your awareness is key. And that's something we have discovered the last... I would say 10 years, put a lot of effort into work on attention skills and how to develop a flexible focus system in you so that you are able to focus on what you want to do, instead of being caught in the moment and not being able to show us what you can do when the pressure is on. Because that will be the case, that you will experience a lot of pressure and you will have to be able to handle that situation also when you feel very little confidence or afraid or whatever feelings you have, you still have to be able to focus on what you should do. And that's where we would put a lot of effort into training that ability. How do you train that ability? We use different approaches. We do, of course, mindfulness-based approaches. That's something that the research from Anders Meland came about. That was really important in our perspective. But we also work with values. Who do you want to be? Who do you want to become? What kind of athlete do you want to be? Do you want to be the brave one? Then you can't do the shortcuts. So we kind of have to work on several abilities or factors that are important when it comes to mental skill training. We also work on how you can distance yourself from some of the thoughts that might steal your focus at some points. So you can diffuse from the setting. "I'm not my thoughts. So I can still do it even though I think I'm... I feel not confident." That's a feeling, but I don't have to think that I am not confident. I have these thoughts that I'm not confident. And that's a huge difference. When you distance yourself a bit from your thoughts, you can free yourself to still do what you want to do. You can do the tasks you have set out to do, even though you don't really feel like you would do it in the first place. But then you start to master things and then confidence kind of becomes a result of your focus. Would everybody benefit from that type of focus training, you think? I think so, for sure. Because it brings you back to the moment, to here and now. And there's a lot of things we can do even though we don't feel like doing it. And to kind of separate the two frees us in many ways. And when we start doing things, kind of mastery follows very often, or experience follows, and then we can learn from that instead of sitting and waiting for a confident feeling before we start doing things. And that, I think, is a huge shift in mindset, that you start acting instead of just waiting for a feeling to come before you actually do the action. You also work with artists and people in the creative industry. What are the similarities? Oh, I have learned so much from them. Because they have to perform every night and give something to the audience every night. And I have seen how extraordinarily well they prepare. They go through these rehearsals time and time and time again, and it's even more impressive than some of the athletes, I would say. They prepare into minor details; they're focused on details. So... and the similarity, of course, is connected to what also we do in sport. And I tend to work with artists that are interested in sport. So I can use some of the analogies and of course I am an expert in the sport area. So they have to kind of see what is transferable for their performance and their kind of life. But they also find that they don't have this big support system that athletes have. So it has been a huge pleasure to be able to work with some of the artists, for sure. You think business people are a bit dull? Yes. Well, I think it's more for me, it's more fun to work with athletes and performers. Why? What is so dull with business people? Well, you know, when we win an Olympic gold medal or if you see a huge performance, you can cheer and you can jump and you can cry and you can laugh. You have a lot of emotions. And you can cry with athletes when they don't succeed, because that very often happens as well, that they do not succeed. So I think the emotional span within the performing arts and also in sport is much larger. So I don't know what you do here when you have a good day but... Well we don't, we don't really dance around. No, I can imagine. So... but and I like dancing around when something good is happening. So it's I guess it's just me. I feel that that's my area of competence is within sport. So and I have a huge pleasure working with so determined young people who really want to achieve their goal, and being maybe able to shape that kind of road a bit for them and help them maybe reach what they want to do. It's a huge privilege, I would say. So do you think it'll be a better place if we introduce more emotions and celebrations and high fives here? I think... I think we should celebrate good results somehow in some way. And you need to find, you know, I'm a researcher, so when we have a published paper, we do a little "Hey" in the office. So it's not much celebration there either. So I think you need to kind of tailor it to where you are. But some kind of celebration I think is in place because it's what we're working for, isn't it? To try to achieve our goals and then get some inspiration to continue to work hard. I saw this quote from this famous tennis player, "Pressure is a privilege." We use that also a bit. Yeah, and we've started to use it too, because it is... the fact that you have pressure means that you have an interesting job oftentimes, right? For sure. For sure. Do the best actually like pressure? I think... well, I know some of them are terrified just before they are going to dive into whatever they are supposed to do. But at the same time, the award afterwards is quite huge when you have mastered it. You've been able to do what you have done in practice so many times and then being able to do it when it really counts is a huge feeling of mastery. So and that feeling is difficult to experience anywhere else I think in that kind of magnitude, in some ways. So yeah, it's a privilege. We try to put a lot of effort into gratitude. Just in Livigno now when we were down there in Italy, looking at the mountains, we stayed in this nice hotel place in the Olympic village with a spectacular view. And we just had to say, you know, thank you for being here. I mean this is we can't take this for granted. So we need also to ground ourselves and be really grateful for what we have. Yeah. What does gratitude do to people? Oh, even science has kind of found that this is hugely important for our brain, to also to live good lives. And of course it creates a kind of atmosphere when people are grateful instead of taking things just for granted. It's very different. So it creates a humbleness in that moment where you also should be very courageous. So it's kind of again a span of emotions coming there. And so I think it's, yeah, it's important. I read a book last year called The Gratitude Diary. And so I start my day every day, first five minutes, just going through all the things I'm grateful for. Yes. And it puts you in a different place. It does. It also reminds you that you can't take things for granted. And in the last few months in the sports community, there have been some quite tough situations which we had to cope with. And then of course you get a realization check of what you can't take for granted. Tomorrow you can have an injury, for example, that takes you out of sport and things can happen. So now we should be in the moment and be grateful for what we have. And I know I've been to many Olympics; I don't know how many more I will attend. And I was reminding myself this last Olympic that I'm so privileged to work with these people and these coaches who really put everything on the line to help these athletes perform. So there's so many people here in this kind of support system that also put a lot of effort into creating these performances. If you look at the most important values amongst the people you work with, what do you think are the finest values you see? Caring for each other. And I see it so much in the two winter sports I've had the honor to follow now, which are free skiing and snowboard. And then you see it across cultures as well. So when somebody is landing a difficult trick, maybe something nobody has done before, the whole community cheers. Different countries just cheer this person who has done this trick. And I've never seen that in any other sports, except maybe in orienteering, which I'm also following, where people really are happy about people who succeed regardless of which country you're from. So to see that kind of... that people care for each other, I think it is a huge thing. Then they share, they help each other, and they coach each other. And of course that's very powerful when you have a lot of expertise together and they share; that's when magic happens. How do you bring that attitude into a company or into a business? I think you need to have a quite secure environment. You need to feel that psychological safety. And I think that's actually a key, that I know that even though I come with this kind of maybe critique or issue, it doesn't come back to me. You don't stab me in my back and I don't lose my place on the team. I'm part of a community where you actually can share also things that are not functioning that well. So you need to create that kind of environment within the group. And if it's not there, then you need to start there, I think. And how do you create it? You need to have a coach or a leader that actually wants to do this and is not afraid of having athletes coming with their opinions. And I think that's where we maybe in Norway are more open to having athletes who have an opinion. We want them to take ownership and be part of the development of the sport. If you have coaches coming from different cultures coming to Norway, they maybe find that a bit difficult—that you have kind of outspoken athletes who want to have their say in decisions. So that had to be taught and had to be cultured in many ways, that we are secure enough to have that kind of environment. Then you have to have coaches who have a lot of skills also because they are more secure instead of thinking that they have to know it all. What's the key to creating good teams? We know that the coach or the leader is important; he sets the tone. I think it also has to be part of different kind of personality styles within a team. You need to have a few rules that you need to attend to and follow up on. Because otherwise, everybody's just doing their own business. Rules such as what? What are the important rules? Could be such as that we don't have the phone when we have a meal. We eat together sometimes, and if we share an apartment, we need to try to have it a bit tidy. We make dinner together. Quite ordinary, simple rules, not too many but enough to show respect for each other, that we are not just alone in here. And this can take time, I can tell. But how do you build a team consisting of people who actually compete against each other? Yeah, that's a bit different because it's... and we have learned a lot actually from the alpine. So like the downhill team, right? They compete with each other, try to beat the shit out of each other when they compete, but at the same time they train together and so on. Yeah, one of my colleagues from a different country said, "That would never happen in my country." And but again, what they have said and which I think most of us try to replicate is that they say, "Yes, we are competitors, of course, but we also know that if we as a team evolve and are successful, it helps all of us to be more successful." So they kind of... and if I don't win, I want you to win, not someone from Sweden or Denmark or somewhere else. So we kind of try to stick to that. Are we sure that a more aggressive approach wouldn't have given us more medals, you know? Starting earlier, get you into a sport, put everybody with big hands into swimming, you know, put all the tall people into basketball, and just totally go for it. I'm sure we would have had some who would succeed in that model as well. But we would have lost so many others, which would have been such a shame. So I think it's a gamble to do that. Of course, if you have a huge amount to pick from, it might be successful, but I still would say that you maybe would lose the biggest talent that way because you wouldn't be able to spot them at that time. We know so much about those who succeed, that a lot of them have succeeded quite late—15 or 16—and they would have not been part of that kind of selection that you are mentioning there. So I think we just would have lost so many, and it wouldn't be... I don't think it would have been that much fun. The key thing we have to do with the young athletes is to keep them motivated and keep them in sport. And then when they're kind of 15, 16, or 17, we will know who really wants to put in that effort. And the rest of them would have had a joyful ride and they will be good friends with those who become the stars. So I think there's so much to gain. And also the physical aspect which I think we see more and more. There's a lot of injuries in sport, and maybe it's also due to the fact that they are not so well prepared now as they were just 10 years ago. Because then they were a lot more out in the street playing, climbing trees, falling down, and building this kind of ability to handle all the training they have to do later on. So there's so many reasons why being a bit more patient is a success criteria. So Anne Marte, I'm now making you the head of the Norwegian Sovereign Wealth Fund. Nice. And what would you do with this organization? Just how would you think about it? You inherit now a team of professionals. Just what are the kind of things you'd be thinking about to develop an organization? I think I would have done exactly what you did: hire a sports performance consultant or someone in sports psychology. So I think that is a key. Of course, that's my bias, but I think it's important that we also focus on that part of development. But otherwise, I would have had to hire a lot of really skilled people because I wouldn't have a clue of how to work with that kind of organization. But in terms of building the team and building the mentality, what would be the important things? Oh, a lot of communication, openness, welcoming new ideas. New people have new ideas and we need to listen to them and also to try to create a happy environment. Because it's much more fun to work when you feel safe. And safe doesn't mean you don't have to do anything, but safe in the way that you have my back if something goes wrong. The ability to be able to also make mistakes. You learn a lot from them if you are willing to kind of share them. So trying to create that kind of climate, I think, would be a key thing. So I would lean on the kind of research we have in those kind of environments and try to build that one part at a time. There seems to be sometimes a bit of misunderstanding with what psychological safety is, in that people also think it means job security even if you are a total loser, which is not what it is. No. Not at all. So what is it? No, it is that I feel secure enough to say when I disagree with something. I actually feel I can say that without you punishing me one week later and taking me out of that team and preventing me from doing my job. So it's a delicate question. It's difficult, of course, as a leader. Nobody really likes to be criticized or told that the approach you have chosen is wrong. So we also need to create a culture where you share that disagreement in an okay manner so that you manage to keep the communication going. Because if the communication stops flowing, problems will start to arise really quickly. You can never take that for granted. A good culture can die in a second if the trust is not there anymore. So it's a huge challenge to work on that. It's never done. You have to work on it, keep working on it, and be open for change all the time. Lastly, what is your advice to young people, not only in sports but generally speaking? How should you think about your life? Well, I think a key thing is to find something you really find is joyful, a place where you can laugh, where you can have a lot of fun, where you have good friends, and where you feel that you're welcome. Every child and youth should be part of an environment like that, whatever it is. It could be in the performing arts; it could be in sport. But being part of a community that kind of cares for you is so valuable, and I think it can help you through all the kind of challenges you will meet in life, especially as a young person. You should have good friends around you. So if I should pick a sport, I would look for the one where the climate is good and you have a nice coach and there's a lot of laughter on the training. A lot of content people. That's so important because without motivation, you will never perform anything. You need that culture to take care of that. And I assume that translates into studies and work choices as well. Yeah, I have a lot of athletes who ask for career advice and I say, "Well, my only advice is to pick something you really love doing." If you like this book or read on this topic, maybe that's kind of a path you should follow. I honestly feel that I haven't worked in my entire life because I have just done what I really am eager to do. So that's the only advice I can give. Not to be too strategic and sensible, but just go for it. Probably a bad advice, but that's my advice anyway. I think it's a very good advice. You clearly followed it yourself and have had great results. Big thank you. Thank you very much.

Horacio Marín (YPF) on Argentina Energy, Vaca Muerta & Global Investment | IEFA Latam Forum 2026

IEFA TV(37.0K) ·27:23 ·1 views · 2026-03-27
Horacio Marín (CEO, YPF)
YPF is leveraging Vaca Muerta’s shale potential to transform Argentina into a global energy powerhouse, targeting $45B–$50B in annual industry exports by 2031.
TL;DR YPF is leveraging Vaca Muerta’s shale potential to transform Argentina into a global energy powerhouse, targeting $45B–$50B in annual industry exports by 2031. CEO Horacio Marín is prioritizing operational efficiency, massive infrastructure projects like "Vaca Muerta Sur,"...

TL;DR

YPF is leveraging Vaca Muerta’s shale potential to transform Argentina into a global energy powerhouse, targeting $45B–$50B in annual industry exports by 2031. CEO Horacio Marín is prioritizing operational efficiency, massive infrastructure projects like "Vaca Muerta Sur," and a $100B LNG partnership to drive a 3x–4x increase in company EBITDA.

Strategic Operations and Market Pricing

  • "Honest and Moral" Pricing Policy: Marín emphasized a commitment to transparency with consumers, stating that YPF will not speculatively hike domestic fuel prices during transitory global spikes (e.g., oil exceeding $100).
      • Prices will only be adjusted to reflect real cost impacts on YPF, maintaining a steady margin rather than taking advantage of geopolitical volatility.
  • Efficiency Gains through Shale: YPF has shifted focus from conventional to unconventional (shale) resources, resulting in $1.3B in operational cost savings last year.
      • The company generates an "extraordinary" $22.50 per barrel in the domestic market due to high efficiency.
      • Shale oil production is projected to reach 215,000 barrels per day this year, a 120% increase over 2023.
  • Investment Portfolio: YPF plans to invest approximately $6B this year, with $4.2B dedicated exclusively to Vaca Muerta.

The "Argentina GNL" (LNG) Megaproject

  • Scale and Partnerships: Leading a massive LNG consortium with Eni (Italy) and ADNOC (Abu Dhabi).
      • The project is four times larger than competing regional proposals, aiming for 12 million tons of production.
      • Total lifecycle investment is estimated at $100B over 20 years, with $30B in immediate infrastructure and upstream development.
  • Financing and Global Interest: JP Morgan is acting as the Project Financial Advisor.
      • Initial soundings showed interest from 47 financial institutions, with appetite reaching 2.4x the required funding.
      • Marín clarified that while Santander is a likely participant, no exclusive $9B deal has been signed yet; the project remains in the documentation phase.
  • Strategic Advantage: Argentina is positioned as a secure, conflict-free energy source. Marín noted that Argentine LNG can reach Asian markets more competitively than U.S. exports due to geographic positioning and access to both the Atlantic and Pacific.

Key Infrastructure: Vaca Muerta Sur

  • Eliminating Bottlenecks: The "Vaca Muerta Sur" pipeline is essential for moving shale oil to export markets.
      • The pipeline is currently 54% complete, with the river crossing expected to finish in April.
      • Export Timeline: Mechanical completion is slated for October 2026, with the first exports expected by Q1 2027.
  • Massive Scale: The storage tanks at Punta Colorada are compared to the size of football stadiums (diameter) and 10-story buildings (height), each holding nearly one million barrels.
  • Collaborative Investment: For the first time, these projects are being funded by private industry collaboration rather than state spending, which Marín argues is a fundamental shift toward a more efficient economic model.

Long-Term Economic Vision

  • Export Powerhouse: By 2031, Marín predicts YPF will be the largest exporter in Argentina across all industries.
      • Industry Target: Total energy exports (oil, gas, and LNG) are expected to reach between $45B and $50B annually.
  • Regulatory Support: Marín credited the national government’s RIGI (Incentive Regime for Large Investments) as the essential catalyst for these projects.
      • "Without RIGI, there is no LNG project," Marín stated, noting that the government has provided the necessary macro stability and legal framework for the private sector to take the lead.
  • Job Creation: The peak of construction (around 2027) is expected to create over 40,000 jobs, driven by the construction of massive 48-inch pipelines and separation plants.
"When it goes up, it goes up; when it goes down, it goes down. When you make an agreement with millions of consumers, it is much stronger than signing a contract. It is what my grandmother told me: 'What you say, you do.'" — Horacio Marín
"By 2031, with LNG included, we will be the company with the highest exports in Argentina of anything you can imagine—not just oil and gas, of anything." — Horacio Marín
"This is the first collaborative project with private investment in infrastructure... the companies are investing, not the 'foolish state' (estado bobo) as some wanted. This change is why there is now investment in Vaca Muerta." — Horacio Marín
And now what we're going to do, we're going to move to the fireside chat, which is... the subjects are energy, geopolitics and investment, and why Argentina matters today. We have one of the leading members of Argentina economy, and he is... he led one of the largest Argentinian companies with great success in the last few years. Tenemos aquí uno de los grandes representantes de la economía y las finanzas de Argentina. Ha liderado los últimos años una de las compañías más importantes de Argentina con mucho suceso. Lo vamos a recibir con un fuerte aplauso al señor Horacio Marín, quien es presidente y director ejecutivo de YPF. Thank you so much Horacio for joining us. Y quien va a ser entrevistado en este fireside chat, esta charla informal, por quien es la corresponsal de Reuters aquí en Argentina. Un fuerte aplauso para Eliana Raszewski. — Hola, buenas tardes a todos. ¿Me escuchan bien? Es bastante cómodo el sillón. ¿Me cambio? ¿Usted dice? Bueno, como todos saben, es Horacio Marín, CEO de YPF. Estamos todos ansiosos por escucharlo. Estamos viviendo una etapa histórica con un barril que en las últimas dos semanas subió más de 40% y supera los 100 dólares. Y bueno, no puedo dejar de preguntarle al principio de esta charla cómo afecta esto, cómo beneficia y en qué cambia para YPF este contexto. — A ver, ¿qué queremos nosotros? Acá tengo que explayarme. Hace un año, creo que fuimos... no me acuerdo si televisión y radio, en los dos lados, y dijimos: "Vamos a hacer un acuerdo honesto y moral con ustedes. Cuando sube sube, baja baja y bajamos". Cuando vos hacés un acuerdo con millones de consumidores es mucho más fuerte que firmar un contrato. La palabra esa que decimos "honesto y moral" es lo que me decía mi abuela: "lo que decís, hacés". Y no vamos a especular, porque esa palabra significa "no voy a especular con vos". Entonces lo que nosotros estamos viendo, y la mayoría habla igual, es que hay que diferenciar lo transitorio de lo permanente. Transitorio es especulación, porque no es... si el Estrecho de Ormuz mañana se normaliza, es altamente probable —es lo que piensan todos los analistas— que va a caer el precio del petróleo. Entonces, ¿qué gana YPF en subir especulativamente y bajar luego cuando no tenemos ese impacto? Si yo te hago ganar un montón de plata cuando no tengo un impacto en mis costos, no soy honesto y moral con vos. Entonces ¿qué estamos haciendo nosotros? Vemos día a día el impacto que tiene en YPF, nos mantenemos en el margen que teníamos antes de la guerra y solamente vamos trasladando el impacto en YPF. Para que todos vean, nosotros ¿qué tenemos? Nosotros tenemos 30% que exportamos... perdón, que compramos, pero 10 que exportamos. Nos queda 20% neto de impacto, cuando el otro porcentaje está absolutamente hedgeado en mercado interno. Entonces ¿qué hacemos? Mantenemos el margen que teníamos porque esto es transitorio y vamos aumentando solamente si lo vamos trasladando; es el impacto que tiene en las arcas de YPF. Creo que nos tienen que aplaudir de parado todos los consumidores porque es una de las cosas más honestas que se puede ver. Y eso es lo que estamos haciendo. Entonces, si pasa a ser permanente es otra cosa, es el acuerdo honesto y moral: sube sube, baja baja. Pero en este momento que es transitorio no vamos a tomar ventaja desde el punto de vista especulativo. Creo —no hablo, pero por lo que veo— hay empresas que son competencia nuestra en el mercado de las naftas y del gasoil que creen que lo que estamos haciendo es absolutamente correcto y ellos están haciendo... van siguiendo a YPF. — Pero voy a apuntar a la empresa en sí, no a la relación con los consumidores. Ustedes proyectaban aumentar a 215.000 barriles por día de shale oil este año, que es un 120% más que en el 2023. Bueno, si exportan parte de eso con... — No, no exportamos. Es lo que dije. Nosotros hoy exportamos el 10% de la producción vía Chile, porque hasta que no esté el Vaca Muerta Sur todo lo demás es para consumo interno. The Iran War is Masking Economic Problems: Why Housing is So Expensive | The Weekly Wrap

Steve Eisman(165.0K) ·50:42 ·41.2K views · 2026-03-27
Steve Eisman (Host, The Weekly Wrap) Phillippe Lord (CEO, Meritage Homes)
> **Steve Eisman:** "These patterns signal the beginning of a credit cycle.
TL;DR: Steve Eisman identifies the "beginning of a credit cycle" as major private credit funds restrict redemptions, while Meritage Homes CEO Philippe Lord reveals that local regulatory fees—often exceeding $150,000 per lot—are the primary driver of the U.S. housing...

TL;DR: Steve Eisman identifies the "beginning of a credit cycle" as major private credit funds restrict redemptions, while Meritage Homes CEO Philippe Lord reveals that local regulatory fees—often exceeding $150,000 per lot—are the primary driver of the U.S. housing affordability crisis.

Geopolitical Instability and Market Volatility

  • Iran War Impact: The markets are currently trading almost exclusively on headlines regarding the war in Iran. While President Trump recently postponed strikes on Iranian energy infrastructure for five days to facilitate talks, Iran has rejected a 15-point U.S. ceasefire proposal.
  • Straits of Hormuz: Hopes for a resolution faded after Iran demanded control over the Straits of Hormuz and labeled U.S. negotiation attempts "illogical."
  • Strategy: Eisman suggests the U.S. may be using delaying tactics to prepare for a seizure of Kharg Island, a critical hub for the Iranian economy.

Red Flags in Private Credit

  • Redemption Caps: Several major private credit funds are facing liquidity pressure.
      • Apollo Debt Solutions: Received redemption notices for 11.2% of its $25 billion fund but only honored the 5% cap.
      • Ares Strategic Credit Fund: Similarly capped redemptions at 5% despite receiving requests for 11.6%.
  • Credit Downgrades: Moody’s recently downgraded a $13 billion private credit fund (jointly run by KKR and Future Standard) to "junk" status after non-accrual loans rose to 5.5%.
  • Banking Pullback: Barclays is scaling back asset-based lending to small borrowers following losses, shifting focus only to large corporates.
  • The "Credit Cycle" Thesis: Eisman warns these patterns—mounting bad news followed by tightened underwriting—signal a transition that typically leads to a recession.

The Structural Roots of Housing Unaffordability

  • The "Lock-In" Effect: Since 2016, most homeowners secured very low mortgage rates, effectively freezing the existing home market. This gave new homebuilders a "captive audience," though demand has recently softened due to low consumer confidence.
  • Incentive Programs: Builders like Meritage Homes (MTH) have maintained sales by "buying down" interest rates for customers—subsidizing rates to ~5.5% when market rates are ~6.5%.
  • Regulatory Cost Drivers: Philippe Lord identifies land regulation as the primary reason housing isn't cheaper.
      • Regulatory Fees: In high-regulation states like California, builders pay roughly $150,000 per lot in impact fees, school fees, and permits before construction even begins.
      • Vertical Costs: Higher standards for energy efficiency and flood-proofing, while environmentally sound, have added significant costs that consumers do not necessarily "value" in the purchase price.
  • The Texas Alternative: Texas utilizes cheap municipal financing to subsidize infrastructure for affordable housing, a model Lord believes the federal government should incentivize nationally.

Meritage Homes (MTH) Investment Thesis

  • Valuation: Meritage is currently trading at a discount to tangible book value (~$68/share vs. ~$75/share book value). Eisman views this as an attractive entry point for a profitable, mid-cap builder.
  • Operational Shift: Meritage is moving toward a "land-light" model.
      • Current Status: They currently own ~70% of their land, which is capital-intensive and lowers Return on Equity (ROE).
      • Future Target: They aim to move to a 50/50 split between owned land and optioned land to improve capital efficiency and reach a 15% ROE.
  • Shareholder Returns: The company plans to repurchase approximately 10% of its common shares through 2026.
  • Performance Goals: Long-term targets include 22.5% gross margins and 10% annual revenue growth.

Notable Quotes

Steve Eisman: "These patterns signal the beginning of a credit cycle. Bad news starts to mount, and then lenders begin to tighten underwriting standards... this process can lead to a recession."
Philippe Lord: "In California... we’re paying regulatory bodies close to 100 to 150 thousand dollars per lot just to build in their community. We haven’t even put a pipe in yet... consumers don't value that; they value how many bedrooms and bathrooms the house has."
Philippe Lord: "The reason affordability is so problematic in our country is because local regulatory bodies have no incentive or motivation to help us bring land to the market in an affordable way."
Steve Eisman: "Historically, if you can buy a homebuilder below tangible book value, if you are patient, you will make money."
Hi, this is Steve Eisman, and this is another edition of the Weekly Wrap. This is for the week ending March 27th, but recorded Thursday night, March 26th. In this week's wrap, we will discuss: one, the latest on the war in Iran; two, more news on private credit; and three, we will then cut to an interview with the CEO of Meritage Homes, symbol MTH, a stock I recommended earlier this year. Right now, the only thing the markets care about is the war in Iran, and the news on the war keeps moving at a fast and furious pace. Monday morning, futures were down 100 basis points. But then, President Trump announced that he was postponing the bombing of Iranian energy infrastructure for five days because the US and Iran are talking. Futures soared. At one point on Monday, the market was up over 200 basis points. However, the Iranians denied that there were any talks at all, and the market sold off some. It still closed up 1.15%, but well off its highs. Tuesday after the close, President Trump announced that the US and Iran are negotiating and that the US had submitted a 15-point plan to end the war. Wednesday morning, Iran rejected this ceasefire proposal and called US talks, quote, "illogical," unquote, and demanded control over the Straits of Hormuz. By Thursday, hopes for a peaceful resolution seemed to be fading. Now, it's possible that President Trump has just been using delaying tactics to set up a time for the US to take over Kharg Island, which is the key to Iran's economy. In related news, on Wednesday, Barclays announced that it is scaling back its asset-based lending to small borrowers after facing losses from the collapses of Market Financial Solutions and Tricolour Holdings. Barclays is shifting its focus to loans and securitizations for large corporates and has already pulled back on some deals and increased pricing to reflect higher risks. If you take one thing away from this wrap, focus on this. These patterns signal the beginning of a credit cycle. This is what it looks like. Bad news starts to mount, and then lenders begin to tighten underwriting standards and to restrict credit to, at first, certain borrowers and more borrowers over time. Because 20 years ago, we could deliver these houses for less and now we can't. And what are the main drivers behind that? And it's just it's three things: it's land cost, it's vertical cost, and it's labor. And so all three of those have been pressured in the last 20 years for different reasons. I'll work backward. Labor is obviously constricted by immigration right now. Not a lot of people who are graduating from college want to get into homebuilding, so we don't have enough plumbers in the world, and we don't have enough electricians in the world, we don't have enough framers, foundation people. And whether it's lumber or wiring or plumbing fixtures or cabinets, flooring, you name it, all those costs have gone up substantially due to supply chain issues and product constraints, tariffs, yadda yadda yadda. And then just the fact that the way we're required to build a home as a new homebuilder is so much more is to such a higher standard than what existing home stock is built to, whether it's code or the windows we put into the house or how we frame a house. The regulatory bodies, the powers that be, have required new homebuilders to continue to increase the cost of their homes to build our homes to a much higher standard." Prior to the Great Financial Crisis, all the homebuilders with the exception of NVR literally would buy the land that they would eventually build on. That land would sit on the balance sheet, and it would generally be the biggest part of the balance sheet. Post-Great Financial Crisis, at least the large homebuilders have migrated more towards the NVR model where instead of buying land, they option more of the land. That has proven to help their ROE because therefore they are tying up less capital in land, they only tie up the capital as it comes closer to entitling the land. So you buy—you're like 75% you buy the land. "Buy the land, self-develop the land. Correct. The first reason is we were kicking off so much cash through our growth that we had over a billion dollars of cash for four years running, and we just believed that the best use of our capital was to deploy it back into our business versus pay some land banker a substantial lift to option the land back to us. It's not cheap. Taking land off balance sheet is not cheap." Explain the mechanics of why that is not cheap. "Yeah, so the way those capital partners operate, Meritage goes out and finds a piece of land, identifies a piece of land that they want to operate on, figures out what it's going to cost to bring it to the market. We then bring it over to a capital partner, they look at the land, they essentially will step in, buy the land on our behalf, improve the land on our behalf, and then roll the lots back to us over some agreed schedule. They usually charge somewhere between 12 and 14%." 12 and 14% of what? "Of the land. Okay. So they're basically marking it up beyond the purchase price, they're marking it up 12 to 14% back to you." Yes, so you're talking about, depending on the length of the deal, the cost of the deal, anywhere from 250 to 400 bips of gross margin, incremental gross margin cost that will go to a lot option capital partner. "So very, very expensive. So it does bring your R down. And one of the reasons we didn't do it is because we had all this cash sitting on our books and it was very expensive. And also over the last five years, land was taking forever to bring to the market. Schedules were expanded because of COVID, the costs were elevated because of COVID and because of the supply chain. And so whenever those schedules are extended and the cost is extended, that burden just gets bigger and bigger and bigger. So for us, with all this cash sitting on our books, we just didn't think it was the right decision at the time. That being said, eventually you get to a point like the other companies did and now we are where your balance sheet can no longer support the growth you want to achieve on its own. And frankly, if you're trying to improve your efficiency of your land book, you have to start taking some stuff off balance sheet. So on our earnings call, we telegraphed to the street that we're sitting at 30 and our goal is to get somewhere between 40 and 50. I kind of like the window we're in right now because land prices are starting to like get soft because the market's gotten soft, and development costs and schedules are starting to tighten up. It makes it a lot less expensive to introduce a capital partner into our future land book today than it did three-four years ago. So I'm hoping our timing is right, but listen, you look at some of these builders that have taken a lot of land off their books and you listen to their calls and they share with what that's costing them. Take a company like Lennar who is now almost 100% off balance sheet through their REIT mechanism called Millgross. Their margins are really not great. They don't love their margins, and a lot of that is that incremental burden they're taking they're paying to take that land off. So there's no free lunch here is basically what you're saying." "Yeah, and it's all math, right? It's looking at the impact to the R and then looking at the opportunity to, you know, financially kind of look at your E differently. But more importantly, it's just about capital efficiency. If you can get it right and you're bringing land on book when you're ready to build homes, it makes you so much more efficient with your working capital which can drive a lot of other benefits to your P&L." Well, let's finish up. I'm going to give you the last word. If we're here five years from now, what's your hope for where Meritage is going to be in terms of profitability, geography, expansion, etc.? What are your goals here over the next three to five years? "I think number one, we want to be a company that produces some of the strongest returns in the company or in the industry. I think we want our gross margins—our long-term gross margin hurdles are 22 and a half. We're currently closer to 19, 19 and a half. There's a 300 or so bip spread there because of incentives and the things we've all the things we've talked about, so we want to get our long-term core operating margins back to that 22 and a half. We want to have a 15% ROE year over year. We want a bulletproof balance sheet. We are one of the few builders out there that's investment grade. We want to remain investment grade, which means that we have to keep our net debt to cap at or below 20%. I think we can do that. And we want to be one of the top growth builders out there, which means we have to grow our revenue around 10% year over year. I think if we do all that really, really well, I think the numbers are going to look great. I think we're going to gain market share. We're going to be in position to compete with some of the big guys. And, you know, hopefully we can get a multiple north of 1.5. You can do the math on that. If we're trading at .95 right now and we can get to a 1.5, we're going to have a lot of happy shareholders, we're going to have a happy board, we're going to have happy investors. Everyone's going to be happy." And on that happy note, Philippe, thank you very much for your time and best of luck on your goals. "Thank you so much for having me. I really appreciate it." So that was a very interesting interview. Couple of takeaways and some investment conclusions. First of all, this is actually a very well-run company. It focuses on the entry-level home, which is really where Americans really need the most help. It's one of the more efficient homebuilders. It's profitable; it grows book value. What I thought was very interesting about the interview was the question that I asked him as to why are homes in America so much less affordable than they once were. And he basically focused on three things: there's labor costs which have gone up, that's the least important; then there are supply chain issues which have impacted the cost of putting in cabinets and kitchens etc.; but the biggest, biggest, biggest cost is local regulations and how much they have increased the cost of land. One thing that he said which I didn't know which was shocking was that in some regions like in California, just to buy a parcel of land per lot, the regulatory fees are so high that before you do anything, it costs you $150,000 per lot in fees to the local regulators just to get started. That is one of the reasons why housing is so unaffordable in the United States. That's a very difficult problem to solve. Trump administration is trying to deal with it, but as of now they don't have any solution, but they seem to be trying to work on something. What I took away from the interview was that Philippe Lord runs a very good company. It has very good margins; it's profitable in good times and bad. But it has an exceptionally low valuation. And historically, if you can buy a homebuilder below tangible book value, if you are patient, you will make money. The tangible book value of Meritage right now is around $74 to $75 per share, and as of today, March 6th, on the day of the interview, the stock is $68 and change. So if you buy the stock, you're buying the stock at a slight discount to tangible book value, which I think long-term is a good investment. What's going to drive this stock higher in the near-term, I think, are if interest rates come down, which would give a real boost to the spring selling season. That would certainly help. But what I think longer-term is going to help is that some of the large homebuilders have moved away from buying land to optioning them, and that has really helped their ROEs. Meritage, however, stuck to being an old-fashioned company where it bought the land, and they buy 70% of their lots as opposed to a Lennar which options over 90% of their lots. Philippe basically said that they were going to move more towards a 50-50 model. Right now their returns are subpar, 10% or lower. They have had higher returns. But I think as they move more towards an optioning model, the returns of this company are going to increase, and in terms of valuation, you could get at least one and a half times tangible book value, which would be 50% upside. If we get some good times, you could get even more. So I actually think this is a pretty good investment, like I said at the beginning and I'm going to say it again, I own it. President Trump Delivers Remarks at the Future Investment Initiative

The White House(3.0M) ·78:27 ·161.8K views · 2026-03-27
Donald Trump (President of the United States)
> "I've always said NATO's a paper tiger.
TL;DR: President Trump asserts that the United States is currently experiencing its most significant economic turnaround in history, characterized by $18 trillion in new investment and the total military decimation of the Iranian regime’s nuclear and conventional capabilities. ...

TL;DR: President Trump asserts that the United States is currently experiencing its most significant economic turnaround in history, characterized by $18 trillion in new investment and the total military decimation of the Iranian regime’s nuclear and conventional capabilities.

Military Strategy and Regional Security

  • Neutralization of Iran: Trump detailed "Operation Epic Fury" and "Operation Midnight Hammer," claiming the U.S. obliterated Iran's nuclear facilities, navy, and air force.
      • Leadership Vacuum: He stated that Iran’s Supreme Leader and top military commanders are "all dead," leaving the country without leadership.
      • Technological Superiority: Highlighted a recent engagement where U.S. Patriot missiles successfully intercepted 101 out of 101 incoming Iranian missiles traveling at 2,500 mph.
  • Critique of NATO: Trump labeled NATO a "paper tiger," criticizing members like France, Germany, and the UK for refusing to provide immediate military aid during recent operations.
  • Middle East Alliances: Praised the "bravery" of Saudi Arabia, Qatar, the UAE, Bahrain, and Kuwait for standing with the U.S.
      • Abraham Accords: Encouraged the expansion of these accords, predicting that all Middle Eastern countries will eventually join the peace framework.
  • Global Conflict Resolution: Claimed to have "settled eight wars" during his tenure, including long-standing conflicts in Azerbaijan and between India and Pakistan, using tariffs as a tool for de-escalation.

Economic Policy and National Investment

  • Unprecedented Capital Influx: Trump claimed the U.S. secured $18 trillion in new investment within the last 11 months, compared to less than $1 trillion during the previous administration.
  • Tax Reform Incentives: Highlighted the "Great Big Beautiful Bill," which includes:
      • Corporate Benefits: 100% expensing and bonus depreciation for factory construction, allowing for full cost deductions in a single year.
      • Individual Savings: Eliminated taxes on tips, overtime, and Social Security for seniors.
  • Manufacturing Resurgence: Credited high tariffs (previously as high as 145% on China) for forcing international automakers from Japan, South Korea, and Germany to build plants within the U.S.
  • Saudi Investment: Noted that Saudi Arabia has committed to investing nearly $1 trillion in the U.S., including a $142 billion defense sales agreement for F-35 fighter jets and other equipment.

Domestic Security and Immigration

  • Border Enforcement: Called for an immediate end to the Democratic-led shutdown of the Department of Homeland Security (DHS), asserting that 25 million "unvetted" individuals entered the country under the previous leadership.
  • Airport Security: Confirmed the deployment of ICE agents to U.S. airports to assist with security and mitigate delays caused by funding disputes.
  • Mass Deportations: Reaffirmed the administration's commitment to deporting "criminal aliens" and "vicious thugs" to restore domestic safety.

Technological Leadership and Innovation

  • AI Dominance: Stated that the U.S. is leading China in Artificial Intelligence and pledged to remove "political correctness" and red tape that could hinder American AI development.
  • Crypto Superpower: Voiced his intent for the U.S. to be the "undisputed crypto capital," highlighting the "Genius Act" which created a framework for dollar-backed stablecoins.
  • TikTok Acquisition: Mentioned his personal intervention with President Xi Jinping to approve the sale of TikTok to a group of American investors to keep the platform operational.

Notable Quotes

"I've always said NATO's a paper tiger. And I've always said we help NATO, but they'll never help us. And if the big one ever happened... I guarantee you they wouldn't be there." — President Donald J. Trump
"In America we don't fear the future, we invent the future. We have the most brilliant minds and we invite all nations to join us in building the most powerful AI ecosystem on the planet." — President Donald J. Trump
"I've saved millions and millions of people... I'd love my legacy to be made as a great peacemaker because I really believe I'm a peacemaker." — President Donald J. Trump

How AI Is Reshaping The Battlefield | Bloomberg Tech: Asia 3/27/2026

Bloomberg Technology(719.0K) ·22:46 ·15.6K views · 2026-03-27
Shery Ahn (Host, Bloomberg Technology) Gus McLachlan (Senior Adviser, Droneshield) David Ha (CEO, Sakana.ai)
AI has transitioned from a support tool to a primary driver of military strategy, fueling a high-stakes arms race between the U.S.
TL;DR AI has transitioned from a support tool to a primary driver of military strategy, fueling a high-stakes arms race between the U.S. and China where the speed of algorithmic decision-making increasingly challenges the feasibility of human oversight. Competing Development...

TL;DR

AI has transitioned from a support tool to a primary driver of military strategy, fueling a high-stakes arms race between the U.S. and China where the speed of algorithmic decision-making increasingly challenges the feasibility of human oversight.

The U.S.-China Strategic Divide

  • Competing Development Models: The U.S. relies on a market-driven partnership model with private firms like Lockheed Martin, Palantir, and Anthropic. Conversely, China employs a "Military-Civil Fusion" (MCF) strategy overseen by President Xi Jinping to eliminate barriers between the civilian economy and the defense sector by 2049.
  • Technological Paranoia: While the U.S. may lead in military algorithm integration, officials remain in a "state of paranoia" regarding China’s rapid adoption.
      • Facial Recognition: U.S. intelligence assessments suggest the U.S. is currently "very much behind" China in this specific capability.
      • Bureaucratic Friction: Domestic adoption in the U.S. faces cultural challenges and "Pentagon bureaucrats" who slow the transition from traditional to AI-driven warfare.
  • Private Sector Tensions: The standoff between Anthropic and the Pentagon over safety guardrails highlights the friction between tech firm ethics and military requirements for unrestricted capability.

AI in Active Operations

  • Current Deployment: The U.S. Central Command is already using AI in operations against Iran to "crunch data," reducing tasks that once took hours or days to mere seconds.
  • Targeting Assistance: AI is being used to generate "points of interest"—identifying location, elevation, and specific assets—though the U.S. military maintains that these are technically "short of a target" to keep humans in the loop.
  • Operational Scale: In modern air campaigns, forces are prosecuting over 1,000 targets a day. This volume of data, including airborne refueling and early warning orchestration, is now impossible to manage without machine assistance.
  • Project Maven: This foundational U.S. effort, launched in 2017, focuses on computer vision algorithms. While early versions struggled with different terrains (snow vs. desert) and low-resolution objects, the technology has evolved into a primary driver of modern "smart systems."

The "Information Overload" Paradigm

  • Multi-Domain Command and Control (MDC2): Military leaders have moved from having too little information to being "awash" in terabytes of data from drones, satellites, and aircraft. AI is now essential for "machine assistance" to prevent senior leaders from being overwhelmed.
  • On-Device AI: Sakana AI is developing visual language models that fit directly on devices (drones/local assets). This allows for local analysis of multimodal data (land, sea, and air) rather than relying solely on centralized processing.
  • Cognitive Warfare: Sakana AI recently analyzed over one million social media posts for the Japanese government to identify state-backed information campaigns, detecting specific "wait-and-see" strategies used by the Chinese state to influence narratives.

Ethics and the "Human in the Loop"

  • Speed vs. Oversight: The democratic military tradition mandates a "human in the loop," but the speed of modern warfare—such as defending against hypersonic missiles—may necessitate automated responses where humans simply cannot react fast enough.
  • Unpredictability Risks: AI remains "black-box technology." Concerns persist regarding "fat-finger mistakes" and the tendency of large language models to "agree" with a user's prompt, potentially escalating a move toward conflict.
  • Terminal Autonomy: In Ukraine, the "Spider’s Web" operation against Russian bombers demonstrated AI-enabled terminal flight. Drones used an image database to identify and hit vulnerable parts of aircraft without human guidance in the final 1,000 meters.
  • The "Red Button" Dilemma: Future military strategy will require a clear differentiation between decision types: those where trend analysis can be automated and those where a "figurative red button" (human cutout) must be preserved.
"The US started this effort known as Project Maven... to develop AI and put it into the battlefield. This is about fearing that the US is falling behind on advanced technology and that China is able to speed up... decisions may be made at lightning speed, faster than humans can think or react." — Katrina Manson
"For the early part of my military career, one of the big challenges that senior leaders had was not having enough information... Toward the back end of my military career, that whole situation reversed. Senior leaders are now awash with information; in fact, they're probably overwhelmed." — Gus McLachlan
"If you're sitting in a ship, for example, and there's a hypersonic cruise missile heading towards your ship, there might only be seconds before it arrives. We're going to have to trust the machine will know better than us." — Gus McLachlan
Artificial intelligence is becoming an integral feature in nearly every system we use, and the defense sector is no exception. It's gaining some of the most cutting-edge capabilities, but that also raises major concerns, especially over the ethical application of the technology. Yeah, that's right. These worries include potentially lethal decisions being made without human involvement, privacy violations around surveillance and data security, and even as these concerns are being debated, AI is already at the center of this new arms race. You've got the two global superpowers at the forefront, and that is China versus the US. As armed conflicts become more frequent around the world, AI is increasingly present on the battlefield. From surveillance and intelligence gathering to autonomous weapons systems. Anthropic's dispute with the Pentagon over guardrails for its systems has brought the debate about military use of AI further into the open. The race to dominate AI defense tech largely pits the United States against China, though each has a different strategy for winning. The United States takes a market-driven partnership approach to working with defense contractors like Lockheed Martin and Raytheon, as well as tech firms like Palantir and, until recently, Anthropic. China, on the other hand, follows a so-called military-civil fusion strategy, or MCF. It's a core national policy overseen by President Xi Jinping, with a goal to develop the PLA into a world-class army by 2049. Beijing wants to break down the barriers between its civilian economy and its defense industrial base. The Pentagon says many of China's technological achievements in areas like aviation, drones, and chips are designed with military uses in mind from day one. For the US, taking Anthropic's highly prized technology out of its military hands complicates its strategy to compete against China in AI-driven warfare. The question is, how do both sides manage to transition from AI as a support tool to a technology with a bigger role in decision making? Bloomberg reporter reporter covering tech and national security Katrina Manson joins us now. She's just released her book called "Project Maven: A Marine Colonel, His Team, and the Dawn of AI Warfare," which explores the US development of AI-driven warfare and examines how it's reshaping the balance between the US and China. Katrina, great to have you with us. So, we think of artificial intelligence war as something of the future, but it's actually happening right now. Tell us a little bit about how we're seeing this development, especially within the US military. At the moment, the US military forces running operations against Iran are publicly saying they're using a variety of advanced AI tools to prosecute their operations. That's to crunch data, bringing work down that normally took days or hours down to mere seconds. And also, I've been told by the spokesperson of Central Command to help generate so-called points of interest. That's everything short of a target, but means things like elevation, location, and what precisely is there. So we can definitely see that in these operations, AI is happening. What is different, though, do you think, in the sense that the US military has already been embedding different AI technologies for decades? Why do you think it's being sped up, and what are the ramifications of that as well?
