And in 2017, the US started this effort known as Project Maven, which I explore in my book, to develop AI and put it into the battlefield. This is about fearing that the US is falling behind on advanced technology and that China is able to speed up. It's very much undertaken with the possibility that the US and China may one day face off in a war and that decisions may be made at lightning speed, faster than humans can think or react. And the problem is that artificial intelligence is not perfect, at least not yet. What are the risks around some of those fat-finger mistakes as well, when you also bring a human into the loop? Yes, I found out that in some of the early cases of algorithmic development, computer vision algorithms really were not up to scratch. They couldn't function in different terrains between snow, desert, and greenery, for example. And they couldn't identify things like a weapon, which can take up only three pixels on an image. Relying on this technology clearly is fraught, but it was, of course, developmental. Things have improved, but I'm told that still, of course, given the research that AI can escalate when turning to a large language model or generative AI and asking it a question, it can also tend to agree. So there is this concern that AI will lead the US and others who use it towards the direction of further conflict. Yeah, that's what I wanted to ask about, because many people are familiar with Clausewitz, his idea of the fog of war, and just talking about the general level of uncertainty. Do you think that with AI technologies and the more than 200 people that you spoke to to write this book, do we see an increase in the fog of war, or does AI actually manage to decrease it? There are two parts to this. For the people really advocating for this technology, they hoped that battlefield information delivered in real time, that could do things as simple and also as hard as identify where the enemy is, where US forces are, could help protect US forces, stave off civilian casualties, and enable faster reaction speeds on the battlefield. But the flip side is that AI can go wrong. It's an unpredictable system, it's black-box technology, and in many cases, the US is asking operators to begin to use it under the hood. So suggestions are surfaced and a US operator must decide whether to accept or reject it. For now, a human is very much making those decisions, but there is an intention to speed up warfare so much that things may ultimately become automated. Yeah, and Katrina, you mentioned, of course, a rivalry between the US and China, and the US potentially feeling that they don't want to fall behind in this technology, fueling the speed here. Where are we at right now? Well, I think the US assessments are classified, but my understanding is that when it comes to facial recognition, the US feels very much behind where China is. When it comes to actually integrating military algorithms into systems, digital systems, and practicing with it, I think the US may feel confident that it has the edge but is in a state of paranoia, concerned that it needs to keep developing. And adoption has been a real cultural challenge for the US within the military all throughout the period of the 10 years I show in the book so far. The team trying to introduce AI has encountered problems, not only from those in wider society who are terrified about what AI will do to the business of war and to the future of human security, but also within the Pentagon. This team was fighting often its notion of what Pentagon bureaucrats were doing to slow them down, and even themselves. There was dissent about whether to work with Palantir, a huge company now in the defense tech space that has partly made its fortune through Maven smart systems, or whether to go another way and really concentrate on delivering top data to algorithms that could sit on drones. Gus, I want to get your take on how much humans can really stay in the loop. This is one of the key questions around how AI is applied and whether you can actually continue to have human decision making that's part of general processes. What's your view on that? Yeah, in my career and I guess in what I would call the democratic military tradition, we expect that a human will always make the final decision. Now, that's easy to say, but if you're sitting in a ship, for example, and there's a hypersonic cruise missile heading towards your ship, there might only be seconds before it arrives. We're going to have to trust the machine will know better than us. And I think for some time in environments like war at sea, machine assistance and algorithms have probably been trusted. Now, in a cluttered urban environment, for example, the war in Gaza, where engagement ranges might only be 100 or 200 meters, we can't just leave machines unchecked to retaliate when we perceive an incoming threat, for example. Certainly that would be the expectation in my career. Now the Anthropic standoff in the US, I think, has occurred because of exactly this issue. People like Hegseth, the new Secretary of the Department of War, he has a view that we should somehow take the gloves off and let these systems work to their absolute potential, with perhaps less consideration for the laws of war. Anthropic and, frankly, most of us in the democratic Western tradition would want those controls in place. Sorting through troves of data and giving us trend analysis is an example. Or decentralized out on that attack drone. We saw the Ukrainian operation Spider's Web that targeted the strategic bomber fleet of the Russian military. We're pretty sure the last thousand-meter flight of those drones was AI-enabled, meaning they had an image of those bombers in their database. They were programmed to identify that image, fly towards and hit the most vulnerable position. You don't need a person in the loop when the image is that clear. So so many uses, we're just going to have to work out where to put the big figurative red button, which is the human cutout button, versus where we will have to automate. Yeah, well we've got... you've already mentioned in the program two very big or very new big advanced technology companies, Palantir and Anthropic. I was familiar with Palantir during my time in service, and they are one of the very first big data analytic platforms that could search across multiple databases. In the global war on terror, for example, we often had individuals having four or five different names, depending on the cultural setting of that name. Tools like the Palantir analytic tool could sort through those and identify that we might be talking about the same individual. Anthropic is the next generation on, and I think the operation that the Americans are conducting now is an example of the speed and complexity that it can ingest and then help them make a more coherent plan. Of course, as it becomes more and more embedded in their systems, once it's inside the ICT security protocols and the personnel security protocols, it becomes more pervasive and harder to dislodge. Inside Marc Andreessen & Ben Horowitz’s Multi-Family Office

Sourcery with Molly O'Shea(31.5K) ·61:00 ·5.0K views · 2026-03-27
Michel Del Buono (CIO, Perennial) Molly O'Shea (Host, Sourcery)
a16z Perennial bridges the gap between retail wealth management and institutional-grade investing by providing tax-optimized, custom portfolios led by professional investors rather than service-oriented bank staff.
TL;DR a16z Perennial bridges the gap between retail wealth management and institutional-grade investing by providing tax-optimized, custom portfolios led by professional investors rather than service-oriented bank staff. Service Providers vs. Investors: Michel Del Buono argues...

TL;DR

a16z Perennial bridges the gap between retail wealth management and institutional-grade investing by providing tax-optimized, custom portfolios led by professional investors rather than service-oriented bank staff.

The Structural Failure of Traditional Wealth Management

  • Service Providers vs. Investors: Michel Del Buono argues that traditional banks and Registered Investment Advisors (RIAs) train staff to be "service providers" focused on growing their book of business rather than "professional investors" focused on alpha.
      • Investment Acumen: In large banks, actual investment expertise is siloed away from the wealth managers who interact with clients.
      • In-House Expertise: Perennial differentiates itself by hiring professional investors who have historically been paid based on their investment performance.
  • Fee Structure Misalignment: Most wealth firms charge a flat Asset Under Management (AUM) fee (e.g., 40–60 basis points).
      • The "Easy Route" Bias: If a firm is paid the same for simple or complex work, they will prioritize simple market betas (stocks and bonds) over sophisticated alternatives.
      • Fund-of-Funds Drag: Firms without in-house investment teams are forced to use third-party managers, creating a dual layer of fees that can reach 3–4% annually on a look-through basis.
  • The "Tax Alpha" Gap: Traditional institutional managers (serving pensions and endowments) ignore taxes because their clients are tax-exempt.
      • Taxable Realities: For individuals in high-tax states like California, optimizing for after-tax returns is the "easiest alpha" to capture.

The Case Against Single Family Offices (SFOs)

  • Scale and Talent Challenges: Unless a balance sheet is in the billions, the cost of hiring a team of 5–7 specialists across asset classes (venture, real estate, credit) often outweighs the benefits.
      • Retention Issues: High-end professionals often avoid SFOs because there is no clear career path and they frequently work in a vacuum away from the principal.
  • Fragility and Longevity: Statistics show SFOs frequently collapse after the patriarch or matriarch passes away.
      • Liquidation Risks: Heirs often liquidate assets in a hurry, resulting in significant losses or the complete dissolution of the investment strategy.
  • Perennial’s Minimums: Del Buono suggests that while $25M–$50M is a baseline, their model is specifically designed for "centimillionaires and billionaires" with institutional, multi-generational time horizons.

Strategic Asset Allocation and "Tax Alpha"

  • Real Estate as a Core Pillar: For taxable individuals, real estate is highly attractive due to its lack of correlation with tech stocks and significant tax benefits.
      • Depreciation Benefits: If held long-term, investors can use depreciation credits to receive income while paying minimal taxes.
  • Monetizing Volatility: Rather than viewing market swings as a threat, Perennial views volatility as a "fat pitch" or a "blue light sale."
      • Concentrated Stock Strategy: For founders with massive positions in companies like SpaceX, Del Buono advises against immediate dumping. Instead, they use options programs to monetize volatility without exiting the core position.
  • Liquidity Buffers: Cash and Treasuries are held not for their 3–4% return, but for the "option value" to pivot into distressed or high-growth assets during market drawdowns.

The Realities of Taxes and "The California Exit"

  • Draconian Moves: Del Buono describes moving to Florida or Texas as a "draconian" life change that is often harder than founders anticipate.
      • The Residency Test: To avoid California taxes, one must truly sever ties—selling the principal residence, changing physicians, and registering to vote elsewhere.
  • Structuring Over Moving: Founders can often mitigate tax burdens through sophisticated prep before a liquidity event.
      • QSBS and Trusts: Utilizing Qualified Small Business Stock exemptions and creating multiple trusts can significantly shield gains without requiring a physical move.
      • The "Boomerang" Effect: Many who move for tax reasons eventually return because the personal cost of the lifestyle change outweighed the percentage points saved.

Navigating Private Markets and Secondaries

  • Venture Capital Dispersion: VC has the widest performance gap between the best and worst managers of any asset class.
      • Entity Value: Del Buono highlights that a16z is built to have "entity value" that survives its founders, offering institutional infrastructure (HR, go-to-market, legal) that smaller "solo-GP" firms lack.
  • Secondary Market Risks: The rise of Special Purpose Vehicles (SPVs) for companies like SpaceX or Anduril has created a "dark pool" of potentially questionable investments.
      • The "Molly Corp" Trap: Founders may put stock into a personal entity and sell shares of that entity. Investors often lack true legal claim to the underlying cap table.
      • Due Diligence: Perennial performs background checks on SPV managers and expensive legal reviews to ensure ownership interests are "perfected."
"Banks themselves don't train people to be professional investors. These people are trained to be service providers. They're trained to be responsive, helpful, but actual investment acumen when you're at a large bank sits in a separate group." — Michel Del Buono
"Volatility is not the enemy. If you have a deep balance sheet and you've kept your portfolio at least somewhat liquid, volatility is a huge opportunity. It's like a fat pitch." — Michel Del Buono
"We don't hire people for lack of flaws, we hire people for their skills." — Marc Andreessen & Ben Horowitz (as cited by Del Buono)

So the flat fee arrangement, you know, means that people do not invest in building out these alternative teams. It's a lot of work, it's a lot of effort, and it's a lot of expense. And if you make the same 40, 50, 60 bips doing that or just buying stocks and bonds, you're going to buy stocks and bonds, right? So by and large, that industry I think is not teed up for a sophisticated portfolio that is deserving of someone that's got 50, 100, 200 million let alone a billion dollars, right? So that's one route. And then the other route is the traditional institutional asset management route. And those guys, their biggest clients are nonprofits, right? And they are not, these institutional asset managers, because most of their clients are non-taxable, they're not even attempting to optimize after-tax return. In fact, you could even argue that from a fiduciary perspective, they're not allowed to optimize after-tax return because the vast majority of their clients care about pre-tax return. So they're structurally not able to serve you. So there's this weird no-man's land where you have taxable individuals that have and deserve sort of an institutional quality portfolio build and asset allocation, and yet neither of the two standard channels really deliver that. It's a valid point. Chamath recently went viral for a tweet about capital losses because of the destruction his SPACs have incurred on people. What is your viewpoint on capital losses and how do you use them, to your point of offset? So we use them a lot, right? Capital losses are the core behind all those sort of liquid strategies I was mentioning to you before, the leverage strategies and whatnot. There's some nuances in the tax code like you can't use a loss that comes to you personally unless you happen to be a professional in that industry. So you can only use a small part of them. So the way to use a loss is inside a fund vehicle that generates its own gains that can be offset by its own losses. Then you don't have to worry about extracting the losses and putting them on your personal balance sheet and not being able to use them. So if you owned a SPAC or something like that and it went to zero, you may not be able to write that off, right? But if it's sitting in a portfolio in a fund structure, usually a partnership, you're going to have other things inside that fund that are going to be offsets naturally so that you can shield the total result of that fund from tax. And that's exactly I think the right strategy for real assets and real estate, where you're generating these capital losses, you're generating depreciation losses, and you use those to offset the income inside that fund. And then the total you get at the end has been tax-mitigated. Right? SPACs were a huge trend. Secondaries have taken storm. I want to do a quick fire on some macro. Okay. Is private credit in a bubble? There's several flavors of private credit. One is sort of direct lending. So the idea is FICO scores and things like that aren't the best indicators of credit quality. Maybe we can develop a different way to assess credit quality and we'll lend directly to an individual often, okay? That stuff has been fairly resilient, and in part because it's tranched out. So they sort of take the equity tranche, sometimes called the first loss piece, and they keep that on their balance sheet—the issuer does—and then the other pieces are sold, so there's like a buffer, right? If there's a few defaults, you're okay. Right? The... but there's more the sort of private credit lending to companies, mainly sponsor-backed companies. And when people say sponsor-backed, they mean PE-backed companies. And remember the PE companies, how do you... PE company, private equity, they're equity investments, right? So how do you maximize return on equity? Well, you use as little equity as you can. Right? So you buy a company and you lever it as much as you can so that your equity piece is small, so that if the company improves, the return on equity is magnified, right? And so you're purposely levering these companies quite highly, and you're putting that into a vehicle and people are investing into that leverage. So if there's an economic headwind and those companies start not being able to manage their debt, that's a problem. Right? So at the end of the day, the question of whether or not private credit is in a bubble is really a question of: Are we facing some sort of long-term slowdown or recession where these companies can't service their debt the way they were before? Anthropic is currently outperforming OpenAI in enterprise execution and product velocity, while the broader tech market faces a "SaaS-pocalypse" as investors re-evaluate software moats in an era of AI-driven digital abundance.

The AI Frontier: Anthropic’s Momentum vs. OpenAI’s Shift

  • Anthropic’s "Generational Run": The company is firing on all cylinders with Claude 3.5 and 4.6, specifically winning the enterprise market through its focus on coding and agentic workflows (e.g., Computer Use).
      • Revenue Structure: Chamath notes a "RevRec" (Revenue Recognition) difference: OpenAI is 75% consumer subscriptions and 25% API, while Anthropic is the inverse.
      • Regulatory Capture: David Sacks expressed concern over Anthropic’s "permissioning regime" strategy in D.C., suggesting their push for AI regulation creates moats that block new entrants.
  • OpenAI’s Strategic Pivot: Rumors of "panic mode" at OpenAI coincide with the cancellation of the Sora video app’s $1B Disney deal and a newfound focus on chasing Anthropic into the enterprise sector.
      • Private Equity Model: OpenAI is reportedly offering PE firms guaranteed returns (17.5%) to help deploy AI and manage high upfront costs.
  • The "Model Council" Advantage: Perplexity is highlighted for its model-agnostic approach, allowing users to compare outputs from various LLMs simultaneously to identify differences in reasoning.

The "SaaS-pocalypse" and AI Valuation Resets

  • Valuation Compression: Chamath presents a "Years to Payback" analysis showing SaaS giants like Snowflake and ServiceNow have seen their valuations cut significantly (e.g., Snowflake from 100x free cash flow to 50x) as the market fears AI disruption.
  • Superintelligence vs. Next-Gen Software: A critical investment fork exists:
      • If AI is just better software, current multiples may hold.
      • If "Superintelligence" is coming, almost all current software moats become fragile and prone to "strangulation-as-a-service," where AI agents bypass complex UIs entirely.
  • The "HALO" Strategy: Friedberg advocates for High Asset Low Obsolescence (HALO) investments—physical "atoms" that AI cannot easily disrupt, such as natural gas (Cheniere), mining, and space infrastructure (SpaceX).
  • The Death of Brands: Chamath argues that brands may go to zero in an era of abundance, as consumers prioritize "cheaper, faster, better" utility over brand affiliation, citing Tesla’s disruption of legacy luxury auto brands.

Meta’s Landmark Legal Defeats

  • Verdict 1 (Child Safety): A New Mexico jury ordered Meta to pay $375 million for failing to protect minors from predators on Facebook and Instagram.
  • Verdict 2 (Addictive Design): An LA jury found Meta and YouTube negligent for designing addictive platforms that harmed a user’s mental health, navigating around Section 230 protections by using "product liability" language.
  • The "Tort Tax": Friedberg argues that the U.S. "tort tax" costs the economy $900 billion annually (3% of GDP) and criticizes the erosion of personal and parental responsibility in favor of corporate litigation.
  • Parental Responsibility: The "besties" debate the difficulty of policing social media use when schools require devices (Chromebooks) that serve as backdoors to addictive content like YouTube Shorts.

PCAST and the Industrial Race with China

  • New Appointments: David Sacks (Co-chair) and David Friedberg have officially joined the President’s Council of Advisors on Science and Technology (PCAST).
  • The Research Gap: Friedberg highlights a staggering statistic: 10 years ago, China published 50% as many scientific papers as the U.S.; last year, they published 50% more than the U.S.
  • Industrial Science: The new PCAST focus shifts from pure discovery to "builders and doers," aiming to win the industrialization race in biotech, quantum computing, and semiconductors against China.
  • Liquidity Conference: Bill Ackman and Andrej Karpathy were announced as upcoming speakers for the May event.
"We jokingly called it 'strangulation-as-a-service'—enterprises want a shim where they can just write what they need, and the AI deals with the complexity in the background. They never want to see these [complex UI] products ever again." — Chamath Palihapitiya
"We never talk about responsibility. We always talk about where the government failed us and where these companies fucked us. We never talk about what we individually did wrong... Where the fuck was I as a parent?" — David Friedberg
"What if your personal digital assistant gets so good that you don’t need to check your phone? You just tell it what to do, and you don’t need the wall of apps anymore. You could imagine the phone operating system getting disrupted if the agents are good enough." — David Sacks

Their business model makes solving this problem existential. And this is just sort of along the lines of this essay that I wrote. Let me just give you the thought exercise and you guys react. Today we live in a world where the whole market is trying to debate what is the PE ratio that you'd be willing to pay. So Facebook is incredibly durable, I'm willing to pay 30 times. Nvidia is really durable, I'm willing to pay 40 times. Tesla is incredibly asymmetric to the upside, I'm willing to pay 200 times. And then Caterpillar or John Deere, I'm willing to pay 15 times. I'm just using these as an example. And what it's effectively signaling to you is how durable all of these cash flows are. And all we do in the public markets when we make an investment is we're just guessing when do the cash flows run out. And we try to say, "Well, here's how much it's worth and here's how much I'd be willing to pay for it today." And what is anything worth in year 10 or year 15 or year 20? Because if you have infinite abundance and you have all this creativity, won't all companies be disrupted? And won't we be in this constant churn of everything getting disrupted all the time? And if you were faced with that problem in the public markets, how would you react? I think the canary in the coal mine are the SaaS stocks. Yes, we jokingly call it the SaaS-pocalypse, but I think it's much more important. I think it's a big societal question. How do you view capital markets, how do you view the health of a company in a world where we've been told there's a superintelligence on the horizon? That makes everything much more fragile than it was before. And the market reaction is to put all these companies on a spectrum and they started here in software and they're re-rating everything down. And they're changing the way that things are being framed from price-to-equity to a multiple of just the cash that you have on hand. And I think that has huge implications mostly to Silicon Valley and largely to employees because we all sell the dream. We start a company and we're like, okay, small salary, big equity upside. But that's implicitly saying in 15 or 20 years this thing is going to be worth some gigantic number. But if instead every business gets disrupted every five or six years, all you're going to end up with is just the cash. And so what should employees do? Tort litigation, you know, costs our economy $900 billion a year in the United States. $900 billion a year. That's how much is spent on the litigation costs, the settlements, and the judgments. It's 3% of GDP and it's growing roughly 10% per year. And these civil penalties decided by juries like they're going against big companies like Meta and YouTube, but it's also food companies, restaurants, everything. Anytime there's a window to sue someone and extract value from them, tort firms are all over them. You know, it's called the "tort tax" now in America and it's not just losses paid by the companies because fundamentally when a big company pays out these tort taxes, they're going to invest less, there's less R&D, less product development, costs stay high, there's fewer new product launches and there's all these crazy restrictions on stuff. The AI Divide: Who Wins and Who Gets Replaced | Prof G Markets

The Prof G Pod – Scott Galloway(737.0K) ·61:56 ·42.0K views · 2026-03-27
Scott Galloway (Host, Prof G Markets) Ed Elson (Host, Prof G Markets) Bill Gurley (General Partner, Benchmark)
Bill Gurley discusses the core principles of his new book, *Runnin' Down a Dream*, focusing on how individuals can thrive in a shifting economy.
TL;DR: Legendary venture capitalist Bill Gurley argues that extreme curiosity and high agency are the primary safeguards against AI displacement, while warning that the "institutionalization" of venture capital and "circular" accounting deals are distorting market health and...

TL;DR: Legendary venture capitalist Bill Gurley argues that extreme curiosity and high agency are the primary safeguards against AI displacement, while warning that the "institutionalization" of venture capital and "circular" accounting deals are distorting market health and excluding retail investors.

Career Strategy and the "Learning Loop"

Bill Gurley discusses the core principles of his new book, *Runnin' Down a Dream*, focusing on how individuals can thrive in a shifting economy.

  • The Power of Obsessive Curiosity: Gurley identifies "immense curiosity" as the common thread among successful people. This creates a self-reinforcing "learning loop" where work feels like play.
      • The "Breaking Bad" Test: A true professional in their "lane" will choose to study their field at night rather than engage in passive entertainment.
  • The High-Agency Advantage: In the age of AI, "high-agency" individuals use the technology as an accelerator, whereas the 59% of workers who are "ambivalent" or "indifferent" (quiet quitting) are at the highest risk of replacement.
  • Strategic Positioning: Gurley emphasizes three specific tactics for career growth:
      • The Epicenter: Move to where the action is (e.g., Silicon Valley for tech, LA for film) to maximize optionality.
      • Peer Groups: Avoid "sharp-elbowed" competition; success is often found in groups that rise together.
      • The Give-Back Mindset: Start mentoring and helping others early in your career to create a positive feedback loop.

The Institutionalization of Venture Capital

Gurley and Scott Galloway contrast the venture landscape of 1999 with the current environment, noting a shift from "Little Tech" to "Big Tech" establishment.

  • Preemptive Funding and "Mega-Rounds": Unlike the early 2000s, where companies went public with $1–2M in quarterly revenue, winners today ingest $400M–$500M minimum before considering an IPO.
  • The Decline of Public Markets: The number of public companies in the U.S. has fallen by over 50% from its peak.
      • Capturing Growth: Late-stage funds now "intercept" growth years that used to occur in public markets, essentially stealing economic upside from retail investors to benefit a small group of institutional LPs.
  • "Circular Deals" and Bad Accounting: Gurley expresses deep concern over "souped-up" revenue models where major players (Nvidia, Microsoft, Google) invest in startups that then use that same capital to buy the investor's services.
      • The Nvidia Multiple: Gurley suggests Nvidia’s multiple may be suppressed because savvy investors recognize these circular deals create "low-quality" or "cashless" revenue.

AI Regulation and "Doomerism"

The discussion explores how the tech industry’s relationship with Washington D.C. has evolved from distance to deep intertwining.

  • Regulatory Capture: Gurley is uneasy about young AI companies "begging for regulation." He views this as a tactic by incumbents to create moats against "Little Tech" (two-person startups).
  • Engineered Fear: Gurley notes that 80% of Americans fear AI compared to only 20% in China, attributing this to "doomerism" promoted by AI leaders like Anthropic’s CEO to spur regulatory intervention.
  • The "Break-Up" Solution: Gurley argues that if market failure occurs due to excessive rent-seeking, breaking up big companies is a better solution than regulation. Regulation is often written by the incumbents themselves, further ensconcing their power.

Market Outlook and Autonomous Vehicles

The speakers analyze specific sectors and the broader economic climate.

  • The "Gini Coefficient" of VC: Scott Galloway notes that venture returns are increasingly clustered among a tiny number of iconic firms (e.g., Sequoia, Benchmark, Kleiner Perkins) that hold "custody" of the best entrepreneurs.
  • Autonomous Vehicle (AV) Stack: Gurley argues that Uber’s network effect remains its strongest asset. If the autonomous "stack" becomes a commodity (or open-source), the network wins. If one vendor gets significantly ahead, they may disrupt the entire model.
  • The Sunk Cost of Education: Gurley warns parents against the "resume arms race," noting that many students are burnt out by age 22. He advises young people to stay open-minded, noting that 10 years post-graduation, a high percentage of people no longer work in their college major.

Notable Quotes

"The best way to inoculate yourself against AI risk is to be the most AI-enabled version of yourself you can possibly be. If you're the one in your group who knows the most about what AI can do, you're the least at risk." — Bill Gurley
"You shouldn't be able to move cash from your balance sheet and create revenue on your income statement... It's bad accounting, and it's unfortunately happening across the spectrum." — Bill Gurley
"If we have companies that are extracting excessive rent for extremely long periods of time and have really, high margins, it's indicative of market failure rather than market success." — Bill Gurley
"VCs recognized they had custody of the relationship with the entrepreneur... they maintain the returns until all the returns have been squeezed out, and then they foist their shit on the public markets as a last stop." — Scott Galloway
